Defining Divine Power: Attribute Affecting According to Will
Defines power and excludes knowledge and nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of divine power (qudra) as an attribute that affects according to will, and what it excludes.

Core Idea
Divine power is not arbitrary; it acts according to will, excluding blind nature and inert knowledge.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif presents the foundational definition of divine power. Before exploring its implications, the author establishes what power is and what it is not. Understanding this definition is crucial for distinguishing between different types of causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Note that it begins with 'In the definition of power: it is an attribute that affects according to will.'
This is the core definition.
2
Identify the first exclusion
Find the phrase 'so excluded that which does not affect, like knowledge.' Understand why knowledge is excluded.
Knowledge is mere disclosure, not an effect.
3
Identify the second exclusion
Find 'and also excluded that which affects but not according to will, like nature for elemental simples.' Understand that nature acts by necessity, not choice.
Nature includes heat, cold, etc.
4
Study the clarifications
Read the commentator's remarks on knowledge and nature. Note that knowledge can be effective in another sense (e.g., God's knowledge), and that 'nature' is not applied to composites.
These clarifications prevent misunderstandings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في تعريف القدرة وهي صفة تؤثر على وفق الإرادة فخرج من هذا التعريف ما لا يؤثر كالعلم، إذ لا تأثير له وإن توقف تأثير القدرة عليه، وخرج أيضاً ما يؤثر لا على وفق الإرادة كالطبيعة للبسائط العنصرية. قوله : ( كالعلم) أي من حيث أنه علم فإنه مجرد الانكشاف بهذا الاعتبار، فلا يضر كونه مؤثراً بوجه آخر كعلم الواجب بما هو كمال وضد بترجيح وجوده على عدمه إرداة . قوله : ( كالطبيعة إلخ ) مثال لما يؤثر لا على وفق الإرادة كالحرارة والبرودة ولذا لم يتعرض الشارح هاهنا لبيان معنى الصفة. قوله: (للبسائط قدرها لما سيجيء من أن الطبيعة لا تطلق في المركبات.
In the definition of power: it is an attribute that affects according to will. So excluded from this definition that which does not affect, like knowledge, for it has no effect even if the effect of power depends on it; and also excluded that which affects but not according to will, like nature for elemental simples. His statement: (like knowledge) i.e., insofar as it is knowledge, it is mere disclosure in this respect, so it does not harm that it is effective in another respect, like the knowledge of the Necessary (al-Wājib) as perfection and opposite by giving preponderance to its existence over non-existence as will. His statement: (like nature etc.) is an example of what affects not according to will, like heat and cold, and therefore the commentator did not address here the explanation of the meaning of the attribute. His statement: (for simple bodies [limā?] their [qadrahā?]) because it will come that nature is not applied to composites.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage defines power (qudra) as an attribute (ṣifa) that affects according to will (al-irāda). It then explains what this definition excludes: first, anything that does not affect at all, such as knowledge ('ilm), because knowledge merely discloses and does not produce an effect, even though power's effect may depend on it. Second, anything that affects but not according to will, such as natural qualities (al-ṭabī'a) in elemental simple bodies (al-basā'iṭ al-‘unṣuriyya), which act by necessity, not choice. The commentator then clarifies that the exclusion of knowledge is from the perspective of knowledge qua disclosure, but knowledge can be considered effective in another sense, such as God's knowledge which, by being a perfection, may influence creation. The example of nature includes heat and cold, which affect naturally. The commentator notes that the author did not discuss the meaning of 'attribute' here, and also notes that 'nature' is not applied to composites.

Key Terms
قدرة
صفة
إرادة
علم
طبيعة
بسائط عنصرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowledge is excluded because it has no effect whatsoever.
Correction: The text clarifies that knowledge is excluded only insofar as it is mere disclosure; it can still be effective in another respect, like God's knowledge which gives preponderance.

Checkpoint
What does the first definition of power exclude?
A) Anything that does not affect
B) Anything that affects not according to will
C) Both A and B
D) Only knowledge

Key Takeaway
Power is an attribute that affects according to will, excluding both inert knowledge and necessitating nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's power, do you truly believe it acts according to His perfect will, or do you sometimes imagine it as a blind, impersonal force? How does this distinction affect your trust in His decisions about your life?
?
This lesson teaches that knowledge alone has no effect—it must be accompanied by will. Where in your own life are you merely 'knowing' what is right without willing to act? What specific step can you take today to move from knowledge to action?
?
The definition of power excludes nature—things that act by necessity without choice. In what areas of your life are you acting like a 'natural force', simply reacting out of habit or compulsion rather than making a conscious, willed decision to obey God? Will you name one habit you will break by an act of will today?

Second Definition of Power: Proximate Principle and Correct Number of Aims
Defines power as proximate principle and corrects the number of aims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second definition of power as a proximate principle and why the correct number of aims in this type is thirteen, not fourteen.

Core Idea
Power is a proximate principle; the eleventh aim is a Mu'tazilite branch, not part of the fourth type.

This lesson examines the second definition of power (qudra) as a 'proximate principle of diverse acts.' The commentator explains why the term 'proximate' is essential to avoid conflating power with its principle. Additionally, a textual correction reduces the number of aims from fourteen to thirteen, as the eleventh aim belongs to Mu'tazilite branches.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its clarification
Read the sentence defining power as a proximate principle. Notice how 'principle' is specified as the effective agent and 'proximate' is a safeguard.
2
Understand the consequence of omitting 'proximate'
If 'proximate' were omitted, the principle of power would itself be power, leading to the absurdity that the Necessary Existent is power because He is the principle of all power.
This shows the logical necessity of the attribute 'proximate'.
3
Identify the correction of the number of aims
The passage mentions that instead of fourteen aims, the correct number is thirteen. The eleventh aim is actually a branch of Mu'tazilite thought, not part of the fourth type.
This is a textual critical note.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the commentator emphasizes the distinction between the branches of the Mu'tazila and the authentic aims.

وقيل : القدرة (ما هو مبدأ قريب للأفعال المختلفة، والمراد بالمبدأ هو الفاعل المؤثر والقريب احتراز قوله : (مبدأ قريب للأفعال المختلفة صرح بالمبدأ القريب إشارة إلى أنه مراد وتركوه بناء على أنه المتبادر من مطلق المبدأ، إذ لو لم يكن مراداً يلزم أن يكون مبدأ القدرة قدرة فيكون الواجب تعالى قدرة لكونه مبدأ لجميع القدرة . قوله : ( أربعة عشر بل ثلاثة عشر ( الأول بالنظر إلى ما وقع في النسخ، والثاني بالنظر إلى ما سيحققه من أن الصواب أن المقصد الذي جعل حادي عشر ليس من مقاصد النوع الرابع، بل من فروع المعتزلة . قوله : ( إذ لا تأثير (له وتأثير علم الله تعالى عند الحكماء بالنظر إلى أنه قدرة ذاته . قوله : ( كالطبيعة للبسائط العنصرية) سيأتي أنَّ الطبيعة هي الصورة النوعية للبسائط، وإنما لم يتعرض لحديث شمول الصفة إياها حتى يظهر الاحتياج إلى إخراجها بالقيد الأخير، كما تعرض لمثله في قوله : فالنفس الفلكية قدرة على التفسير الأول لجواز أن يحمل قوله : كالطبيعة على التنظير لا التمثيل. النوع الرابع - المقصد الأول : تعريف القدرة عن البعيد الذي يؤثر بواسطة كالنفوس الحيوانية والنباتية فإنها مبادئ لأفعال مختلفة مثل الإنماء والتغذية والتوليد، لكنها بعيدة لكونها مبادئ لها باستخدام الطبائع والكيفيات هكذا قيل، وفيه بحث لأنَّ المؤثر في هذه الأفاعيل إن كان هو الطبائع والكيفيات دون النفوس النباتية والحيوانية كانت هذه النفوس خارجة بقيد المبدأ، لأنه الفاعل وإن كان المؤثر فيها هو النفوس وكانت الطبائع والكيفيات آلات لها لم تخرج بقيد القريب لأنَّ الفاعل القريب، قد يحتاج إلى استعمال الآلة: وقد يقال معنى استخدامها إياهما أنها تنهضهما للتأثير في هذه الأفاعيل وبهذا الإنهاض أشبهت الفاعل كالقاسر في الحركة القسرية فإنه يسخر طبيعة المقسور للتحريك، فكانت بحسب الظاهر داخلة في المبدأ وخارجة بالقريب ( فالنفس الفلكية قدرة على) التفسير ( الأول)، لأنها تؤثر على وفق الإرادة، وهذا إنما يصح إذا حملت الصفة على ما يتناول الجوهر والعرض معاً؛ كتناول القوة إياهما، أو يراد بالنفس الفلكية ما يكون
،
قوله : ( وقد يقال إلخ) أي في الجواب من البحث المذكور وهو جواب باختيار الشق الأول وحاصله أنَّ النفوس منهضة للقول والكيفيات كأنها فاعلة لها فكانت داخلة بقيد القريب بالنسبة إلى إخراج النفوس قيد احتياطي، يفيد أنَّ المراد بالمبدأ الفاعل المؤثر حقيقة لا ما يعمه وما يشبهه، فلا يرد أنَّ الألفاظ في التعريفات محمولة على ما هو المتبادر منها من المعاني الحقيقية ما لم يصرف عنها صارف ولا شك أن المتبادر من الفاعل ما هو فاعل حقيقة لا ما يشبهه . قوله: (فإنه يسخر إلخ الفاعل في الحقيقة للحركة هي الطبيعية القسرية باعتبار القوة من القاسر، أو نفس القوة مع أنه يقال للقاسر أنه فاعل الحركة القسرية، باعتبار أنه المستفادة كالفاعل في إنهاضه للطبيعة لتلك الحركة . قوله : ( على ما يتناول إلخ ) بأن يراد بقولهما الصفة ما يقوم بغير أن يكون وجوده مشروطاً بوجود الغير سواء كان متقوماً به أو لا. قوله : (خارجة بقيد المبدأ لأنه الفاعل وتعميم الفاعل من المؤثر بواسطة إنما يفيد إذا كانت النفوس هي المؤثرة في الطبائع والكيفيات. قوله : ( كالقاسر إلخ) يعني أن حركة الحجر المرمي إلى فوق تنسب إلى الرامي، وإن كان فاعل الحركة في المشهور هو الطبيعة المسخرة، فإن قلت : قد سبق من الشارح في بحث الميل أنَّ المبدأ للحركة القسرية قوة استفادها المقسور من القاسر، وثبت فيه زماناً إلى أن يبطلها مصاصات وكلامه هاهنا يخالفه لأنَّ طبيعة الماء المتحرك إلى حيز الهواء بالقسر مثلاً ليست قوة مستفادة من القاسر قلت : طبيعة المقسور تحركه بواسطة قوة استفادتها من القاسر، فيمكن أن يجعل المبدأ الطبيعة، وإن جعل تلك القوة فلا مخالفة.
And it was said: power is a proximate principle (mabda' qarīb) of diverse acts, and by 'principle' is meant the effective agent, and 'proximate' as a safeguard. His statement: (proximate principle of diverse acts) he specified the proximate principle as an indication that it is intended, and they left it (unqualified) because it is the immediate understanding from the absolute term 'principle'; for if it were not intended, then the principle of power would be power, and the Necessary would be power because He is the principle of all power. His statement: (fourteen, but rather thirteen) the first with regard to what appears in the copies, and the second with regard to what he will verify, that the correct view is that the eleventh aim is not among the aims of the fourth type, but rather from the branches of the Mu'tazila. His statement: (has no effect) and the effect of God's knowledge according to the philosophers is with regard to it being the power of His essence. His statement: (like nature for elemental simples) it will come that nature is the specific form of simples, and he did not address the issue of the attribute's inclusiveness of them so that the need to exclude them by the final condition becomes apparent, as he addressed similarly in his statement: 'the celestial soul is power according to the first interpretation' because it is permissible that his statement 'like nature' be taken as analogy, not as an example. The fourth type - first aim: definition of power. Concerning the remote (principle) which affects through mediation like the animal and plant souls, for they are principles of diverse acts such as growth, nutrition, and reproduction, but they are remote because they are principles of them by using natures and qualities. This is what was said, but there is a discussion: if the effective agent in these acts is the natures and qualities without the plant and animal souls, then these souls are excluded by the condition of 'principle' because it is the agent; but if the effective agent in them is the souls and the natures and qualities are instruments for them, they are not excluded by the condition of 'proximate' because the proximate agent may need to use an instrument. It may be said that the meaning of their using them is that they urge them to affect these acts, and by this urging they resemble the agent, like the compeller in forced motion, for he subjugates the nature of the compelled for moving. So they are, apparently, included in the 'principle' and excluded by 'proximate'. (So the celestial soul is power according to) the first interpretation, because it affects according to will. This is valid only if the attribute is taken to include both substance and accident, like the inclusion of 'power' for them both, or if by 'celestial soul' is meant that which is...

His statement: (And it may be said etc.) i.e., in answer to the mentioned discussion, and it is an answer by choosing the first alternative, and its summary is that the souls urge the (natures and qualities) as if they are agents of them, so they are included by the condition of 'proximate' - with respect to the exclusion of souls being a precautionary condition, indicating that what is meant by 'principle' is the effective agent in reality, not what includes and resembles it. So it is not objected that expressions in definitions are taken according to their immediate apparent real meanings as long as there is no reason to divert them, and there is no doubt that the immediate understanding from 'agent' is that which is an agent in reality, not what resembles it. His statement: (for he subjugates etc.) the real agent of the motion is the natural forced motion, with regard to the power derived from the compeller, or the power itself, even though the compeller is said to be the agent of the forced motion, considering him as like an agent in urging the nature to that motion. His statement: (concerning what includes etc.) meaning by his statement 'attribute' that which subsists without its existence being conditioned by the existence of another, whether it is subsistent by it or not. His statement: (excluded by the condition of 'principle' because it is the agent, and generalizing the agent to include the one affecting through mediation only benefits if the souls are effective in the natures and qualities. His statement: (like the compeller etc.) means that the motion of a stone thrown upwards is attributed to the thrower, even if the apparent agent of motion is the subjugated nature. If you say: the commentator has already stated in the discussion of inclination (mayl) that the principle of forced motion is a power derived by the compelled from the compeller, and it remains in it for a time until it is nullified by resistances? and his words here contradict that because the nature of water moved by force into the air, for example, is not a power derived from the compeller. I say: the nature of the compelled moves it via a power it derives from the compeller, so it is possible to make the principle the nature, and if that power is made the principle, there is no contradiction.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the second definition of power as a 'proximate principle of diverse acts' (mabda' qarīb li-l-af‘āl al-mukhtalifa). It then explains that 'principle' means the effective agent and 'proximate' safeguards against remote principles. The commentator clarifies that 'proximate' is specified to avoid the consequence that the principle of power would itself be power, making God's essence a power. A side discussion clarifies that the number of مقاصد (aims) in this type is actually thirteen, not fourteen, because the eleventh is from Mu'tazilite branches. The passage then defines the remote principle as animal and plant souls, which act through natures and qualities. A debate follows: if these souls are not the direct agents, they are excluded by the condition of 'principle'; if they are agents using instruments, they are not excluded by 'proximate' because a proximate agent may use instruments. The celestial soul is considered power under the first definition because it acts by will, provided the attribute is taken broadly. The passage concludes with further clarifications about forced motion and the compeller analogy, emphasizing that the agent in reality may be the nature or the derived power, with no contradiction.

قدرة
مقاصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth type has fourteen aims.
Correction: The eleventh aim is not part of the fourth type but from Mu'tazilite branches, so the correct number is thirteen.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator specify 'proximate' in the definition of power?
A) To exclude remote principles
B) To include all principles
C) To indicate that power is not an agent
D) To match the Mu'tazilite view

Key Takeaway
Power is a proximate principle; the eleventh aim is a Mu'tazilite branch, making the correct number of aims thirteen.

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
7 min
Remote Principles and the Celestial Soul
Defines remote principle and discusses the celestial soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why animal and plant souls are considered remote principles, how the compeller analogy clarifies their agency, and the conditions under which the celestial soul qualifies as power.

Core Idea
Animal and plant souls are remote because they use instruments; the celestial soul is power if the attribute includes substance and accident.

This lesson explores the remote principle in the second definition of power. Animal and plant souls are remote because they affect acts through natures and qualities. A debate ensues about whether they are true agents or merely intermediaries. The compeller analogy illustrates how they resemble agents. The celestial soul, acting by will, is power under the first interpretation, provided the attribute is broadly understood.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of remote principles
Read the passage defining animal and plant souls as remote principles. Note the list of acts they produce.
2
Identify the two alternatives in the debate
The passage presents two possibilities: either the natures are the real agents, or the souls are agents using natures as instruments. Understand each.
This is the core of the discussion.
3
Understand the compeller analogy
The compeller in forced motion is like the souls urging natures. The motion is attributed to the compeller even though nature is the immediate agent.
This clarifies how souls can be considered proximate despite using instruments.
4
Analyze the condition for the celestial soul
The celestial soul is power under the first interpretation because it acts by will. But this holds only if 'attribute' includes both substance and accident, or if by 'celestial soul' is meant a broad concept.
This connects to broader theological commitments.

قوله : ( وقد يقال إلخ) أي في الجواب من البحث المذكور وهو جواب باختيار الشق الأول وحاصله أنَّ النفوس منهضة للقول والكيفيات كأنها فاعلة لها فكانت داخلة بقيد القريب بالنسبة إلى إخراج النفوس قيد احتياطي، يفيد أنَّ المراد بالمبدأ الفاعل المؤثر حقيقة لا ما يعمه وما يشبهه، فلا يرد أنَّ الألفاظ في التعريفات محمولة على ما هو المتبادر منها من المعاني الحقيقية ما لم يصرف عنها صارف ولا شك أن المتبادر من الفاعل ما هو فاعل حقيقة لا ما يشبهه . قوله: (فإنه يسخر إلخ الفاعل في الحقيقة للحركة هي الطبيعية القسرية باعتبار القوة من القاسر، أو نفس القوة مع أنه يقال للقاسر أنه فاعل الحركة القسرية، باعتبار أنه المستفادة كالفاعل في إنهاضه للطبيعة لتلك الحركة . قوله : ( على ما يتناول إلخ ) بأن يراد بقولهما الصفة ما يقوم بغير أن يكون وجوده مشروطاً بوجود الغير سواء كان متقوماً به أو لا. قوله : (خارجة بقيد المبدأ لأنه الفاعل وتعميم الفاعل من المؤثر بواسطة إنما يفيد إذا كانت النفوس هي المؤثرة في الطبائع والكيفيات. قوله : ( كالقاسر إلخ) يعني أن حركة الحجر المرمي إلى فوق تنسب إلى الرامي، وإن كان فاعل الحركة في المشهور هو الطبيعة المسخرة، فإن قلت : قد سبق من الشارح في بحث الميل أنَّ المبدأ للحركة القسرية قوة استفادها المقسور من القاسر، وثبت فيه زماناً إلى أن يبطلها مصاصات وكلامه هاهنا يخالفه لأنَّ طبيعة الماء المتحرك إلى حيز الهواء بالقسر مثلاً ليست قوة مستفادة من القاسر قلت : طبيعة المقسور تحركه بواسطة قوة استفادتها من القاسر، فيمكن أن يجعل المبدأ الطبيعة، وإن جعل تلك القوة فلا مخالفة.

Commentary
This passage presents the second definition of power as a 'proximate principle of diverse acts' (mabda' qarīb li-l-af‘āl al-mukhtalifa). It then explains that 'principle' means the effective agent and 'proximate' safeguards against remote principles. The commentator clarifies that 'proximate' is specified to avoid the consequence that the principle of power would itself be power, making God's essence a power. A side discussion clarifies that the number of مقاصد (aims) in this type is actually thirteen, not fourteen, because the eleventh is from Mu'tazilite branches. The passage then defines the remote principle as animal and plant souls, which act through natures and qualities. A debate follows: if these souls are not the direct agents, they are excluded by the condition of 'principle'; if they are agents using instruments, they are not excluded by 'proximate' because a proximate agent may use instruments. The celestial soul is considered power under the first definition because it acts by will, provided the attribute is taken broadly. The passage concludes with further clarifications about forced motion and the compeller analogy, emphasizing that the agent in reality may be the nature or the derived power, with no contradiction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A proximate principle cannot use instruments.
Correction: A proximate agent may use instruments, as shown by the compeller analogy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes animal and plant souls remote principles?
A) They do not affect acts
B) They affect through the mediation of natures and qualities
C) They affect without will
D) They are not agents

Key Takeaway
Animal and plant souls are remote principles using instruments; the celestial soul qualifies as power under certain conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read about animal souls using natures as instruments to produce acts. What 'instruments' do you rely on in your daily life to perform acts of worship or obedience? Are you the true agent, or are you merely the compeller of habits? Examine one specific act of devotion and ask: am I the soul actively using my faculties, or is it just nature moving? Today, choose one act and perform it with full awareness that you are the agent using instruments solely for Allah's sake.
?
The passage argues that a proximate agent may use instruments and still be considered the agent. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes deny your agency by blaming 'nature' (your temperament, circumstances, biology) for your shortcomings? Identify one instance where you have made an excuse using 'I can't help it' or 'That's just how I am'. Now, see yourself as the agent who can urge that nature toward obedience. What is one concrete step you will take today to override a natural inclination for Allah's pleasure?
?
The celestial soul is considered power only if the attribute includes both substance and accident. This forces you to think deeply about what 'power' means in your own life. Do you define power as the ability to force outcomes (like a compeller) or as the ability to act with will and purpose? Which definition aligns with your daily choices? Challenge yourself: in the next difficult decision, act not out of compulsion or habit, but out of conscious will for Allah's sake, and note the difference in your heart.

Definition of Qudra: Far and Near Principles
Jurjani defines qudra and explains the far vs near principle with a research and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defines qudra (divine power) by distinguishing between far and near principles and how this distinction resolves potential objections.

Core Idea
Divine power is defined by its effect: near principles act directly, far through intermediaries.

In this lesson, we explore the definition of qudra (power) as presented by al-Jurjani. He clarifies that a principle can be 'far' (ba'id) if it acts through intermediaries, like animal souls using natures, or 'near' (qarib) if it acts directly. A research examines whether souls are truly agents or merely instruments, and the answer affirms that souls elevate natures, thus falling under the near principle. This distinction is crucial for understanding how divine power relates to created causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Focus on how the definition of qudra is introduced and the problem of the far principle.
2
Identify the research (bahth)
Find the point where the author presents two possibilities: either the natures are the true agents, or the souls are the agents using natures as instruments. Note how each option affects the classification of souls.
3
Understand the answer and forced motion analogy
The author chooses the first possibility and explains that souls 'elevate' natures, making them resemble agents. The analogy of forced motion (thrown stone) illustrates how a compeller is called an agent even though the immediate mover is the nature.
This resolves the apparent contradiction and clarifies the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد الأول : تعريف القدرة
عن البعيد الذي يؤثر بواسطة كالنفوس الحيوانية والنباتية فإنها مبادئ لأفعال مختلفة مثل الإنماء والتغذية والتوليد، لكنها بعيدة لكونها مبادئ لها باستخدام الطبائع والكيفيات هكذا قيل، وفيه بحث لأنَّ المؤثر في هذه الأفاعيل إن كان هو الطبائع والكيفيات دون النفوس النباتية والحيوانية كانت هذه النفوس خارجة بقيد المبدأ، لأنه الفاعل وإن كان المؤثر فيها هو النفوس وكانت الطبائع والكيفيات آلات لها لم تخرج بقيد القريب لأنَّ الفاعل القريب، قد يحتاج إلى استعمال الآلة: وقد يقال معنى استخدامها إياهما أنها تنهضهما للتأثير في هذه الأفاعيل وبهذا الإنهاض أشبهت الفاعل كالقاسر في الحركة القسرية فإنه يسخر طبيعة المقسور للتحريك، فكانت بحسب الظاهر داخلة في المبدأ وخارجة بالقريب.
قوله : ( وقد يقال إلخ) أي في الجواب من البحث المذكور وهو جواب باختيار الشق الأول وحاصله أنَّ النفوس منهضة للقول والكيفيات كأنها فاعلة لها فكانت داخلة بقيد القريب بالنسبة إلى إخراج النفوس قيد احتياطي، يفيد أنَّ المراد بالمبدأ الفاعل المؤثر حقيقة لا ما يعمه وما يشبهه، فلا يرد أنَّ الألفاظ في التعريفات محمولة على ما هو المتبادر منها من المعاني الحقيقية ما لم يصرف عنها صارف ولا شك أن المتبادر من الفاعل ما هو فاعل حقيقة لا ما يشبهه . قوله: (فإنه يسخر إلخ الفاعل في الحقيقة للحركة هي الطبيعية القسرية باعتبار القوة من القاسر، أو نفس القوة مع أنه يقال للقاسر أنه فاعل الحركة القسرية، باعتبار أنه المستفادة كالفاعل في إنهاضه للطبيعة لتلك الحركة .
قوله : ( على ما يتناول إلخ ) بأن يراد بقولهما الصفة ما يقوم بغير أن يكون وجوده مشروطاً بوجود الغير سواء كان متقوماً به أو لا.
قوله : (خارجة بقيد المبدأ لأنه الفاعل وتعميم الفاعل من المؤثر بواسطة إنما يفيد إذا كانت النفوس هي المؤثرة في الطبائع والكيفيات. قوله : ( كالقاسر إلخ) يعني أن حركة الحجر المرمي إلى فوق تنسب إلى الرامي، وإن كان فاعل الحركة في المشهور هو الطبيعة المسخرة، فإن قلت : قد سبق من الشارح في بحث الميل أنَّ المبدأ للحركة القسرية قوة استفادها المقسور من القاسر، وثبت فيه زماناً إلى أن يبطلها [مصاصات؟] وكلامه هاهنا يخالفه لأنَّ طبيعة الماء المتحرك إلى حيز الهواء بالقسر مثلاً ليست قوة مستفادة من القاسر قلت : طبيعة المقسور تحركه بواسطة قوة استفادتها من القاسر، فيمكن أن يجعل المبدأ الطبيعة، وإن جعل تلك القوة فلا مخالفة.
The fourth type - first objective: Definition of power. Concerning the remote [principle] that acts through an intermediary, like animal and plant souls, for they are principles for different acts such as growth, nutrition, and reproduction, but they are remote because they are principles for them by using natures and qualities – thus it is said. In it there is a research because if the effective agent in these acts is the natures and qualities and not the plant and animal souls, then these souls would be excluded by the condition of being the principle, because it is the agent; and if the effective agent in them is the souls and the natures and qualities are instruments for them, they would not be excluded by the condition of being near, because the near agent may need to use an instrument. And it may be said: the meaning of their using them is that they elevate them for effecting these acts, and by this elevation they resemble the agent, like the compeller in forced motion, for he subjugates the nature of the compelled for moving. So apparently they are included in the principle and excluded from the near. His statement: (And it may be said etc.) – i.e., regarding the answer to the mentioned research, and it is an answer by choosing the first branch. Its summary is that the souls elevate the natures and qualities as if they are agents for them, so they are included by the condition of nearness; the condition for excluding the souls is a precautionary condition, indicating that what is meant by the principle is the agent effective in reality, not what encompasses it or resembles it. So it is not objected that words in definitions are taken in their primary literal meanings unless a context diverts them, and there is no doubt that the primary meaning of 'agent' is what is an agent in reality, not what resembles it. His statement: (For he subjugates etc.) – the real agent of the motion is the forced nature by virtue of the power from the compeller, or the power itself, while the compeller is called an agent of forced motion by virtue of being like the agent in its elevation of nature for that motion. His statement: (Concerning what encompasses etc.) – meaning by 'attribute' what subsists without its existence being conditioned by the existence of another, whether it is constitutive of it or not. His statement: (Excluded by the condition of principle because it is the agent, and generalizing the agent from the effecting through an intermediary only benefits if the souls are effecting in the natures and qualities. His statement: (Like the compeller etc.) – meaning that the motion of the thrown stone upward is attributed to the thrower, even though the agent of motion in common usage is the subjugated nature. If you say: it has preceded from the commentator in the discussion of inclination that the principle of forced motion is a power acquired by the compelled from the compeller, and it remains in it for a time until [resistance?] nullifies it, and his statement here contradicts it because the nature of water moving to the space of air by force, for example, is not a power acquired from the compeller. I say: the nature of the compelled moves it by means of a power it acquired from the compeller, so it is possible to make the nature the principle, and if that power is made [the principle], there is no contradiction.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage defines qudra (power) and clarifies the distinction between the far (al-ba'id) and near (al-qarib) principles. It begins by explaining that a far principle acts through an intermediary, as animal and plant souls are principles for various acts but are considered far because they use natures and qualities. The author then presents a research (bahth) examining two possibilities: either the natures themselves are the true agents, making souls excluded by the condition of being a principle (as they would not be agents), or the souls are the agents and natures are instruments, in which case souls would not be excluded by the condition of nearness since a near agent may use instruments. The answer adopts the first possibility, arguing that souls elevate (tanhad) the natures, resembling agents; thus they are included under nearness to avoid excluding them. The commentator clarifies that this is a precautionary condition, and the primary meaning of 'agent' (al-fa'il) is the real agent, not metaphorical. He then addresses a potential inconsistency regarding forced motion: the motion of a thrown stone is attributed to the thrower, though the immediate agent is the subjugated nature. He resolves it by explaining that the nature moves by a power acquired from the compeller, so either the nature or that power can be considered the principle. The passage thus guards against misunderstanding the criteria for 'far' and 'near' principles in the definition of qudra.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a near principle must act directly without instruments, but the author shows that a near agent may use instruments.
Correction: The passage clarifies that a near agent (qarib) can use instruments; the key is that the agent itself is the direct source of the act, even if it employs intermediaries.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani defines divine power through the far and near principles, ensuring that created causes do not detract from divine agency by clarifying how agents can use intermediaries.

?
You often attribute your free choices to yourself, but the passage compares created agents to a compeller who only elevates a nature. Are you willing to see yourself as a compeller—a channel for God's power—rather than the ultimate source of your actions? How would this change the way you pray before starting a task?
?

Classification of Powers Under Two Interpretations of Qudra
Jurjani's classification of four powers under two definitions of qudra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Jurjani classifies celestial, plant, animal, and elemental powers under two interpretations of qudra (power) — one based on will and the other on producing multiple distinct acts.

Core Idea
Power is defined either by volition or by multiplicity of actions.

This passage presents Jurjani's classification of various powers — celestial, plant, animal, and elemental — under two distinct interpretations of qudra. One interpretation ties power to willed action, the other to producing multiple distinct effects. Understanding this classification is essential for grasping how powers are attributed to different entities in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations
Read the passage and find the criteria for the first and second interpretations of qudra. The first requires will (irada), the second requires being a near principle for multiple different acts.
The first interpretation is about acting according to will; the second is about producing diverse effects.
2
Examine each type of soul/power
Notice how the celestial soul, plant soul, animal power, and elemental powers are each assigned to one or both interpretations. Look for the reasons given.
The celestial soul has will but only one act; the plant soul has multiple acts but no will; animal power has both; elemental powers lack both.
3
Reflect on the exclusion of elemental powers
Consider why elemental powers (whether as forms or accidents) are not considered power under either interpretation. The key is absence of will and consciousness.

فالنفس الفلكية قدرة على التفسير الأول، لأنها تؤثر على وفق الإرادة، وهذا إنما يصح إذا حملت الصفة على ما يتناول الجوهر والعرض معاً؛ كتناول القوة إياهما، أو يراد بالنفس الفلكية ما يكون صفة للفلك لا نفسه الجوهرية، وإن كان مستبعداً جداً دون التفسير الثاني لأنها ليست مبدأ لأفاعيل مختلفة بل لفعل واحد على نسبة واحدة مع الشعور به، والنفس النباتية هكذا في النسخ المشهورة، وقيل : هو سهو من الناسخ، لما مر من أنَّ النفس النباتية ليست مبدأ قريباً، والصواب أن يقال : والقوة النباتية لكن ما في الكتاب موافق للملخص بالعكس، فإنها قدرة على التفسير الثاني لكونها مبدأ قريباً لأفاعيل مختلفة دون التفسير الأول، إذ لا شعور لها بأفاعيلها، وأما القوة الحيوانية فقدرة على التفسيرين، لكونها صفة موثرة على وفق الإرادة ومبدأ قريباً لأفعال مختلفة، والقوى العنصرية سواء أريد بها ما هو صورة مقومة لها ففي الأجسام البسيطة تسمى طبيعة كالنارية والمائية وفي الأجسام المركبة تسمى صورة نوعية لذلك المركب، كالصورة المبردة التي للأفيون والمسخنة التي للقربيون، أو ما هو عرض قائم بها كالحرارة والبرودة، ليست قدرة على التفسيرين إذ لا إرداة لها ولا شعور.

قوله: (لكن ما في إلخ) يحتمل أن يكون من كلام ذلك القائل، وأن يكون من كلام الشارح، وعلى التقديرين تمهيد العذر من جانب المصنف . قوله : ( كالصورة المبردة ) ولو بالعرض فلا ينافي ما ذكره سابقاً من أنه حار، أو يقال : إنه مبني على اختلاف القولين في الأفيون.

So the celestial soul is power according to the first interpretation, because it acts according to will, and this is only correct if the attribute is taken to encompass both substance and accident together; like the word 'power' encompassing them, or if by celestial soul is meant what is an attribute of the sphere, not its substantial self, although that is very unlikely – not according to the second interpretation, because it is not a principle for different acts, but for a single act in a single manner with consciousness of it. As for the animal power, it is power according to both interpretations, because it is an effective attribute according to will and a near principle for different acts. The elemental powers, whether what is meant by them is a constitutive form – in simple bodies called nature, like fiery and watery, and in composite bodies called specific form of that composite, like the cooling form of opium and the heating form of scammony – or what is an accident subsisting in them, like heat and cold, are not power according to either interpretation, since they have neither will nor consciousness. His statement: (different) they are not of a single pattern. His statement: (but for a single act, which is motion in a single manner without difference in speed, slowness, taking, and leaving, and this is based on that besides motion such as rotation, shape, localization in space, preservation, and others are from the specific form. His statement: (Because it has passed etc.) this is only complete according to the author's description that the condition of near is for exclusion from explanation, so perhaps he says: the plant souls are a near principle because they and nutrition, reproduction, and the powers with qualities are instruments, and the condition of near is to exclude what is the principle of power. His statement: (But what is in the book etc.) it is possible that it is from the speech of that speaker, and that it is from the speech of the commentator, and in both cases it is an excuse on behalf of the author. His statement: (Like the cooling form) even if by accident, so it does not contradict what he mentioned previously that it is hot; or it is said: it is based on the difference of opinions regarding opium. His statement: If the attribute is taken to encompass substance – meaning by it the dependent reality, so it includes substances if they are dependent. His statement: (But) what is in the book agrees with what is in the summary (al-Mulakhkhas) – this is from the speech of the commentator, and his statement: 'and the correct thing' is from the speech of the one who said it, and he is Sayf al-Din al-Abhari, and the summary of what is mentioned in the summary is that the effective attribute is either conscious or not, and in both cases either it is a principle for one act or for many acts. So the first division is the soul...
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage classifies different types of qudra (power) under two interpretations (tafsiran). The first interpretation defines power as an attribute that acts according to will (iraada). The second interpretation defines power as a near principle (mabda' qarib) for multiple different acts (af'al mukhtalifa). The celestial soul (nafs falakiyya) qualifies as power only under the first interpretation because it acts with will but only produces a single act (motion). The plant soul (nafs nabatiyya) qualifies as power only under the second interpretation because it is a near principle for multiple acts (growth, nutrition) but without consciousness. The animal power (quwwa hayawaniyya) qualifies under both interpretations because it acts with will and produces multiple different acts. Elemental powers (quwa 'unsuriyya) – whether they are constitutive forms (like natures or specific forms) or accidents (like heat and cold) – lack will and consciousness, so they do not qualify as power under either interpretation. The commentator then explains each point: the word 'power' (quwwa) can encompass both substance and accident; the celestial soul is usually a substance, so generalizing 'attribute' to include it is not completely unlikely; 'different acts' means not of a single pattern; the plant soul's nearness is defended by noting that it uses natures as instruments; a possible scribal error regarding 'plant soul' is discussed; and the classification from al-Mulakhkhas is summarized. The passage guards against conflating the two distinct criteria (volition and multiplicity) for defining qudra.

Misconception: One might think that all powers that produce multiple acts are the same, but the text distinguishes between volitional and non-volitional multiplicity.
Correction: The second interpretation focuses on multiplicity alone, while the first requires will. Animal power meets both, plant soul only meets the second.

Key Takeaway
The classification of power depends on whether the entity acts with will and whether it produces multiple distinct actions; these two criteria yield four categories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Four Types of Effective Attributes
Al-Ījī enumerates the four types of effective attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how effective attributes are classified into four types based on consciousness and multiplicity.

Core Idea
Effective attributes are either conscious or not, and a principle of one or many acts.

Al-Ījī, drawing from al-Mulakhkhas, presents a precise classification of effective attributes (al-ṣifa al-muʾaththira). The taxonomy uses two dichotomies: consciousness and the number of acts produced. This yields four mutually exclusive categories, each with a distinct example from cosmology and natural philosophy. Let’s examine the classification directly from the source.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the two criteria mentioned at the start.
The passage is concise; each word is significant.
2
Identify the two criteria
The classification uses two binary criteria: shāʿira (conscious) vs. ghayr shāʿira, and mabdaʾ li-fiʿl wāḥid (principle of one act) vs. mabdaʾ li-afʿāl kathīra (principle of many acts). Notice how these combine.
3
List the four types
The passage names four types: celestial soul, elemental nature, animal power, and vegetative soul. For each, infer which combination of criteria applies. Check your understanding with the commentary.
The celestial soul is conscious and a principle of one act, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل المذكور في الملخص أن الصفة المؤثرة إما شاعرة أم لا، وعلى التقديرين إما أن يكون مبدأ لفعل واحد أو لأفعال كثيرة، فالقسم الأول النفس الفلكية، والثاني الطبيعية العنصرية، والثالث القوة الحيوانية، والرابع النفس النباتية، وليس في الملخص دلالة على اعتبار قيد القرب في المبدأ والتأثير والأولى تركه ثم لا يتعين كون النسخة المشهورة سهواً، لاحتمال أن يكون مبنياً على اعتبار أنَّ المؤثر هو النفس النباتية والكيفيات آلات لها، إلا أن يثبت من المصنف أن القرب في التعريف احتراز عن النفوس النباتية إذ لا توجد فائدة للقرب سواه .
The summary of what is mentioned in al-Mulakhkhas is that the effective attribute is either conscious or not, and in both cases either it is a principle of one act or of many acts. So the first kind is the celestial soul, the second is the elemental nature, the third is the animal power, and the fourth is the vegetative soul. And in al-Mulakhkhas there is no indication of considering the condition of proximity (qurb) in the principle and effect, and it is better to omit it. Then it is not certain that the well-known copy is a mistake, because it is possible that it is based on considering that the effective agent is the vegetative soul and the qualities are instruments for it, unless it is established from the author that proximity in the definition is a safeguard against the vegetative souls, since there is no benefit for proximity other than that.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage classifies effective attributes (al-ṣifa al-muʾaththira) into four types. The classification is based on two binary criteria: whether the attribute is conscious (shāʿira) or not, and whether it is the principle (mabdaʾ) of one act or many acts. The author states the summary from al-Mulakhkhas: the first kind is the celestial soul (al-nafs al-falakiyya), which is conscious and a principle of one act. The second is the elemental nature (al-ṭabīʿiyya al-ʿunṣuriyya), which is not conscious and a principle of one act. The third is the animal power (al-quwwa al-ḥayawāniyya), which is conscious and a principle of many acts. The fourth is the vegetative soul (al-nafs al-nabātiyya), which is not conscious but a principle of many acts. The author then notes that al-Mulakhkhas does not require the condition of proximity (qurb) in the principle or effect, and it is better to omit it. He also discusses a textual issue: the well-known copy might not be a scribal error, because it could be based on the view that the vegetative soul is the effective agent and qualities are mere instruments. The condition of proximity would then be a safeguard to exclude vegetative souls from the definition of power.

النفس الفلكية
الطبيعة العنصرية
القوة الحيوانية
النفس النباتية
المبدأ
التأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the classification in this passage, which type of effective attribute is conscious and a principle of many acts?
A) Celestial soul
B) Elemental nature
C) Animal power
D) Vegetative soul

Key Takeaway
Effective attributes divide into four types based on two binary criteria: consciousness and the number of acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson classifies effective attributes into four types. Consider your own actions: are they more like the single, non-conscious effect of fire, or the many, conscious movements of an animal? What does that reveal about your sense of purpose and intentionality?
?
The celestial soul is conscious but produces only one act. Have you ever felt that your life is focused on a single overarching goal? If so, is that goal truly conscious and chosen, and are you as constant in it as the celestial rotation?
?
The vegetative soul produces many effects without awareness. Are there areas of your life where you act without reflection—habits, routines, or patterns—that still produce results? Could bringing awareness to those areas transform them from mere nature into conscious action?

The Condition of Proximity and Textual Variants
Al-Ījī discusses the condition of proximity and a textual variant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition of proximity (qurb) is debated and how a variant reading in al-Mulakhkhas affects the classification.

Core Idea
Proximity may be a safeguard to exclude vegetative souls from the definition of power.

After presenting the four types, the author addresses a subtle point: whether the definition of effective attribute should include the condition that the principle is 'proximate' (qurb). He notes that al-Mulakhkhas does not mention this condition and that omitting it is preferable. Then he discusses a textual variant that might explain why some copies include it. Let’s read the passage and unpack the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the author's stance on the condition of proximity and the mention of a textual variant.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يتعين كون النسخة المشهورة سهواً'.
2
Understand the issue of proximity
The author argues that al-Mulakhkhas does not indicate the condition of proximity (qurb) in the principle and effect, and it is better to omit it. Ask yourself: why might someone include it?
3
Analyse the textual argument
The author considers a possible interpretation: the well-known copy might not be a mistake if it is based on the view that the vegetative soul is the effective agent and qualities are instruments. Proximity would then safeguard against including vegetative souls. Notice how the author leaves the issue open unless the original author's intent is proven.
This shows the depth of kalām textual analysis.

The Definition of Ability and the Issue of Proximity
Classification of forces and critique of including proximity in definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition of proximity (qurb) is not essential in the definition of ability and how the classification of forces relates to this issue.

Core Idea
Proximity is not a necessary condition for defining ability.

In defining ability (qudra), scholars debated whether it must be a proximate source of acts. The passage before us lists different types of forces and argues that including proximity in the definition is unnecessary and better omitted. Let's see the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on forces and proximity
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the list of four forces and the discussion about proximity.
The passage mentions celestial, elemental, animal, and vegetative forces.
2
Understand why proximity is not required
The passage says al-Mulakhkhas does not support proximity, and it is better to abandon it. Consider the alternative interpretation that proximity might exclude vegetative souls, but even then it is not beneficial.
The author leans toward omitting proximity from the definition.
3
Reflect on the classification of forces
Think about how these forces relate to ability: are they sources of acts? This prepares us for the next lesson on diverse acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفلكية، والثاني الطبيعية العنصرية، والثالث القوة الحيوانية، والرابع النفس النباتية، وليس في الملخص دلالة على اعتبار قيد القرب في المبدأ والتأثير والأولى تركه ثم لا يتعين كون النسخة المشهورة سهواً، لاحتمال أن يكون مبنياً على اعتبار أن المؤثر هو النفس النباتية والكيفيات آلات لها، إلا أن يثبت من المصنف أن القرب في التعريف احتراز عن النفوس النباتية إذ لا توجد فائدة للقرب سواه. قوله: ( وليست أفعالها مختلفة ) إذ المراد من كون القدرة مبدأ للأفعال المختلفة، أن تكون مبدأ لفعل تارة أخرى لآخر، وليست القوى العنصرية كذلك، وإن كان تصدر عنها أفعال كاليبس والإحراق من النار مثلاً. قوله: ( القدرة الحادثة على رأينا ) أجاب عنه صاحب المقاصد بأن ليس المراد التأثير بالفعل بل بالقوة، بمعنى أنها صفة من شأنها التأثير والإيجاد على ما صرح به الآمدي، حيث قال: القدرة صفة وجودية من شأنها تأتي الإيجاد والإحداث بها على وجه يتصور ممن قامت به الفعل بدلاً عن الترك، والترك بدلاً عن الفعل والقدرة الحادثة كذلك، لكن لم تؤثر لوقوع متعلقها بقدرة الله تعالى.
Celestial, and second elemental natural, and third animal power, and fourth vegetative soul, and there is no indication in al-Mulakhkhas of considering the condition of proximity in the source and effect, and it is better to abandon it. Then it is not certain that the famous version is an error, because it is possible that it is based on considering that the effective agent is the vegetative soul and the qualities are its instruments, unless it is established from the author that proximity in the definition is a precaution against the vegetative souls, since there is no benefit to proximity other than that. He said: (And its acts are not different) that the meaning of ability being a source of different acts is that it is a source of one act at one time and another at another, and the elemental forces are not like that, even if acts like dryness and burning emanate from fire, for example. He said: (The created ability in our view) answered it by the author of al-Maqasid that the intended [meaning] is not effect in actuality but in potentiality, meaning that it is an attribute whose nature is to effect and originate, as al-Amidi stated, where he said: Ability is an existential attribute whose nature is to effect and originate such that the agent is imagined to perform the act instead of refraining, and refrain instead of performing, and the created ability is like that, but it did not effect because its object occurs by the power of God.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage clarifies the definition of ability (qudra) as a source of diverse acts and addresses an objection about created ability. It first lists types of forces—celestial, elemental natural, animal, and vegetative—and notes that the condition of proximity (qurb) in the definition is not supported in al-Mulakhkhas and is better abandoned. The passage then explains that the requirement that ability be a source of different acts means it must be capable of producing different acts at different times, which elemental forces (like fire that always produces heat) do not meet. Finally, it presents the response from the author of al-Maqasid: the created ability is not defined by actual effect (ta'thir bi al-fi'l) but by potential effect (ta'thir bi al-quwwa), meaning it is an attribute inherently suited to effect and originate, as al-Amidi defined it. This potential is present in created ability, but it does not actually effect because its object is already effected by God's power.

Key Terms
قدرة
قيد القرب
القوى العنصرية
نفس نباتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that proximity is an essential condition in the definition of ability.
Correction: The passage argues that al-Mulakhkhas does not support proximity, and it is better to abandon it. The only possible use is to exclude vegetative souls, but even then it is not beneficial.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is not listed among the forces?
A) Celestial
B) Elemental natural
C) Divine
D) Animal

Key Takeaway
The definition of ability should not include proximity as a condition because it serves no clear purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held on to a religious practice or belief that you later realized had no clear basis or benefit? What would it take for you to honestly re-examine and possibly abandon it, just as the author argues to drop the condition of proximity?

This passage shows the author using careful textual reasoning from al-Mulakhkhas to critique an inherited definition. Are you willing to scrutinize your own religious convictions with the same rigor, even if it means challenging what you have always been taught?
?
The author considers a potential purpose for the proximity condition—excluding vegetative souls—but still deems it unnecessary. Is there anything in your faith that you justify with a 'possible' purpose, even though it lacks clear evidence? Will you commit to seeking truth over convenience?

Ability as Source of Diverse Acts and the Response on Created Ability
Clarification of diverse acts and response to created ability objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that created ability is defined by potential effect rather than actual diversity of acts.

Core Idea
Created ability is potential, not actual effect.

The passage continues by clarifying what it means for ability to be a source of different acts. It then addresses an objection about created ability: if created ability cannot produce diverse acts, how can it be called ability? The answer redefines ability as an attribute of potential effect, not actual.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on diverse acts and the objection
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. First, the passage explains that ability must be a source of different acts at different times. Then an objection about created ability is raised and answered.
Pay attention to the key phrase 'ta'thir bi al-quwwa' (potential effect).
2
Understand the distinction between actual and potential effect
The response distinguishes between effect in actuality (би-л-фи'l) and effect in potentiality (би-л-кувва). Created ability has the potential to effect, but does not actualize because God does so.
This preserves the definition of ability while maintaining divine omnipotence.
3
Reflect on al-Amidi's definition
Al-Amidi defines ability as an existential attribute whose nature is to effect and originate, allowing for the possibility of acting or refraining. This applies to created ability as well.
This definition resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

Defining Power: Consensus and Dissent
Consensus on power as existential and Bishr's non-existential view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mainstream definition of power (qudra) as an existential attribute and the alternative non-existential view advanced by Bishr ibn al-Muʿtamir.

Core Idea
Power is an existential attribute that makes both acting and refraining possible.

Before diving into detailed theology on divine attributes, we need to agree on what 'power' means for created beings. Al-Jurjani lays out the consensus view that power is an actually existing quality, and then presents a dissenting opinion that reduces power to merely the absence of defects. This passage sets the stage for debates on whether power is an added entity or just a condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then the translation. Notice the structure: consensus, then dissenting view, then a famous follower of that view.
Pay attention to the technical terms: 'ṣifa wujūdiyya' and 'ṣifa ʿadamiyya'.
2
Identify the three positions
Find and list the consensus definition, Bishr's definition, and al-Razi's choice. Understand how each defines power.
The consensus says power is existential; Bishr says it is non-existential (absence of defects).
3
Understand the implications of 'existential vs non-existential'
Reflect on why this distinction matters. If power is existential, it is an added attribute; if non-existential, it is merely the lack of obstacles. This affects how we think about God's power and free will.
Bishr's view reduces power to a condition, not a separate quality.
4
Connect to the broader theological context
Consider why al-Razi, a major Ashʿarite theologian, adopted the Muʿtazilite Bishr's view. This shows that even within schools, there is nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتفقت الأشاعرة والمعتزلة وغيرهم على أنَّ القدرة صفة وجودية يتأتى معها الفعل بدلاً عن الترك، والترك بدلاً عن الفعل وقال بشر بن المعتمر: القدرة الحادثة عبارة عن سلامة البنية عن الآفات فجعلها صفة عدمية قال: فمن أثبت صفة زائدة على سلامة البنية فعليه البرهان واختار الإمام الرازي في المحصل مذهبه
The Ashʿarites, Muʿtazilites, and others agreed that power (al-qudra) is an existential attribute (ṣifa wujūdiyya) with which the act is possible in place of refraining, and refraining in place of the act. And Bishr ibn al-Muʿtamir said: The originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) is [nothing] but the soundness of the constitution (salāma al-binya) from defects; thus he made it a non-existential attribute (ṣifa ʿadamiyya). He said: Whoever affirms an additional attribute beyond soundness of constitution, the proof is upon him. And Imam al-Rāzī chose his school in al-Muḥaṣṣal.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage states the agreement of the Ashʿarites, Muʿtazilites, and others that power (qudra) is an existential attribute (ṣifa wujūdiyya), meaning it is an actually existing quality that makes the act possible as an alternative to refraining, and refraining as an alternative to the act. Then it mentions the dissenting view of Bishr ibn al-Muʿtamir, who said that originated power (qudra ḥāditha) is simply the soundness of the bodily constitution (salāma al-binya) from defects, making it a non-existential attribute (ṣifa ʿadamiyya) – i.e., the absence of defects. Bishr challenged: whoever affirms an additional (existential) attribute beyond this soundness must provide proof. The passage notes that Imam al-Rāzī chose Bishr's view in his book al-Muḥaṣṣal.

Key Terms
القدرة (al-qudra)
صفة وجودية (ṣifa wujūdiyya)
صفة عدمية (ṣifa ʿadamiyya)
سلامة البنية (salāma al-binya)
القدرة الحادثة (al-qudra al-ḥāditha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that power is only the ability to act, not to refrain.
Correction: The consensus definition explicitly states that power makes both the act and refraining possible, i.e., it is the ability to choose either.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is Bishr ibn al-Muʿtamir's definition of originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha)?
A) It is an existential attribute enabling action and refraining.
B) It is the soundness of the constitution from defects.
C) It is an additional attribute created by God.
D) It is the ability to act in place of refraining.

Key Takeaway
Power is defined by the majority as an existential attribute enabling both action and inaction, but some reduce it to merely the absence of physical defects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the times you have failed to do something good or succeeded in avoiding harm, do you attribute that to your own power or to a deeper gift from God? How does the existential view of power challenge you to see every action and refrain as dependent on a real attribute given by Him?
?
Al-Razi, a towering scholar, chose a minority view on power. Are there beliefs you hold simply because they are popular among your teachers or peers, without personally examining the evidence? What would it cost you to honestly re-evaluate one such belief today?
?
If power were merely the absence of defects, as Bishr claimed, then you would have no real ability to choose—only the lack of impediment. In your own life, have you ever experienced a situation where your physical health was fine, yet you still could not act virtuously? What does that reveal about the true nature of power?

Knowing Originated Power Through Immediate Awareness
Al-Jurjani presents the Ash'ari proof for originated power via immediate awareness and provides two illustrative examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'aris prove the existence of originated power through immediate awareness (wijdān) and the examples that illustrate this.

Core Idea
We know our power exists by direct experience, not by deduction.

The Ash'ari position on proving human power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) relies on an intuitive awareness that everyone can verify. This lesson introduces the method of immediate awareness and the two key examples of ascending by choice versus falling involuntarily, and voluntary movement versus tremors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: first a statement of the method, then examples.
2
Identify the two examples
The passage gives two examples: ascending vs falling, and voluntary movement vs tremor. Reflect on how each pair shows a distinction that points to an underlying power.
These examples are meant to be self-evident.
3
Understand 'wijdān' as immediate awareness
Recognize that the proof does not rely on abstract reasoning but on a direct, necessary awareness. Ask yourself: Can you directly feel the difference between moving your hand voluntarily and it trembling?
This awareness is what the Ash'aris call wijdān.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : إثبات القدرة الحادثة وتعريفها اختلف في طريق إثباتها أي إثبات القدرة الحادثة والعلم بها والحق ما عليه الأشاعرة وهو أنها تعرف ويعلم وجودها بالوجدان كما أشرنا إليه حيث قلنا: إن الفرق بين الصاعد بالاختيار والساقط عن علو ضروري، فإنا نجد حالة الصعود أمراً ثابتاً لها دون حالة السقوط، وكذا نجد تفرقة ضرورية بين حركة الارتعاش وحركة الاختيار وقال الهمداني من المعتزلة هو أي طريق إثباتها تأتي الفعل قوله : ( وكذا تجد تفرقة ضرورية بين حركة الارتعاش وحركة الاختيار اعترض عليه الإمام بأن الاختيار قبل الفعل باطل عندكم ومعه ممنوع لامتناع العدم حال الوجود وأيضاً حصول الحركة حال ما خلقها الله ضروري وقبله محال فأين الاختيار وأجيب بأن الضروري هو التفرقة بمعنى التمكن من الفعل والترك بالنظر إلى نفس حركة الاختيار مع قطع النظر عن الأمور
The Fourth Objective: Proving the Originated Power and Its Definition. There is disagreement regarding the method of proving it (i.e., proving the originated power and knowing it). And the truth is what the Ashʿarites hold, which is that it is known and its existence is known through immediate awareness (wijdān) as we have indicated, where we said: The difference between the one ascending by choice and the one falling from a height is necessary [and] self-evident, for we find the state of ascending as an established thing for it without the state of falling. Likewise, we find a necessary distinction between the movement of tremor (ḥarakat al-irtiʿāsh) and voluntary movement (ḥarakat al-ikhtiyār). And al-Hamadānī from the Muʿtazila said: The method of proving it is the occuring of the act. He said [the commentator says]: 'And similarly we find a necessary distinction between the movement of tremor and voluntary movement.' The Imām [al-Rāzī] objected: 'Choice (al-ikhtiyār) before the act is invalid according to you, and with it is prevented because of the impossibility of non-existence (al-ʿadam) during existence. Also, the occurrence of movement when God creates it is necessary, and before it is impossible; so where is choice?' And the answer was: 'The necessary [thing] is the distinction in the sense of capability (al-tamakkun) of the act and refraining, with consideration of the voluntary movement itself apart from other matters.'
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage from the fourth section deals with proving the existence of originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) in humans. It states that there is disagreement on the method of proof, and the truth according to the Ashʿarites is that it is known through immediate awareness (wijdān). The proof they provide is the necessary distinction we perceive between ascending voluntarily and falling involuntarily: we find that the state of ascending involves an established factor (power) that is absent in the state of falling. Similarly, we find a necessary distinction between trembling (ḥarakat al-irtiʿāsh) and voluntary movement (ḥarakat al-ikhtiyār). The Muʿtazilite al-Hamadānī instead held that the method of proof is the occurrence of the act itself. Then the commentator records an objection from Imam al-Rāzī: if choice (ikhtiyār) is considered before the act, it is invalid according to you (Ashʿarites?), and if it is simultaneous with the act, then it is prevented because non-existence cannot coexist with existence. Also, the movement occurs when God creates it necessarily, and before that it is impossible, so where is choice? The answer given is that the necessary distinction is in terms of capability (tamakkun) of the act and refraining, considering the voluntary movement itself apart from other considerations (i.e., the capability is what is perceived, not a separate power before the act).

Key Terms
الوجدان (al-wijdān)
القدرة الحادثة (al-qudra al-ḥāditha)
حركة الارتعاش (ḥarakat al-irtiʿāsh)
حركة الاختيار (ḥarakat al-ikhtiyār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the proof relies on observing external acts and inferring a power behind them, but the proof is immediate awareness, not inference.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that we directly find (najidu) the distinction, not infer it.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view in this passage, how is originated power known?
A) By logical deduction.
B) By immediate awareness (wijdān).
C) By revelation.
D) By consensus.

Key Takeaway
Originated power is known through direct, immediate awareness, not through inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a voluntary action—like raising your hand—do you directly sense your own power in that moment, or have you become so habituated that you no longer notice it? This lesson says the distinction is self-evident. If it is not self-evident to you, what has dulled your awareness?
?
Consider a time you acted out of habit rather than choice. Could you have stopped yourself? What does that tell you about the reality of your originated power? Commit today to pause before one voluntary action and consciously acknowledge the power behind it.
?
If your originated power is known only through immediate awareness, what happens when you ignore that awareness? Are you at risk of attributing your actions to mere circumstance? This lesson calls you to own your choices as expressions of God-given power. Will you accept that call?

Objection and Answer: Capability (Tamakkun) as the Basis of Proof
Al-Jurjani records al-Razi's objection to the wijdān proof and the Ash'ari response clarifying capability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's objection to the wijdān proof and the Ash'ari response that the perceived distinction is one of capability (tamakkun) concurrent with the act.

Core Idea
The perceived distinction is not prior choice but simultaneous capability.

After presenting the proof via immediate awareness, the text records an objection from Imam al-Razi and the Ash'ari response. This lesson explains the objection that choice cannot exist before or with the act, and the answer that what is directly perceived is the capability (tamakkun) to act or refrain, which is simultaneous with the movement.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read al-Razi's two-part objection: choice before the act is invalid, and choice with the act is impossible because non-existence cannot coexist with existence. Also, the movement occurs when God creates it.
Notice that al-Razi is questioning where choice fits in.
2
Identify the Ash'ari response
The answer shifts the focus from a separate entity called choice to the notion of capability (tamakkun) that is directly perceived within the voluntary movement itself.
This resolves the objection by redefining what is directly known.
3
Understand capability as concurrent
Capability is not a power existing before the act nor a separate entity during the act, but the very fact that the movement is under one's control is perceived as a distinction. This preserves the immediate awareness proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the answer was: 'The necessary [thing] is the distinction in the sense of capability (al-tamakkun) of the act and refraining, with consideration of the voluntary movement itself apart from other matters.'

This passage from the fourth section deals with proving the existence of originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) in humans. It states that there is disagreement on the method of proof, and the truth according to the Ashʿarites is that it is known through immediate awareness (wijdān). ?

?
This lesson distinguishes between having a prior choice and having simultaneous capability. Which do you normally assume you possess? Take two minutes in silence, examine a recent decision, and ask yourself: Did I really have a separate power, or was it simply the possibility of alternatives? How does this affect your gratitude to Allah for enabling your actions?

Definition of Power and Two Deviant Views
Defines created power and presents two deviant views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of created power and the two minority views that reduce power to limb capability or to the act itself.

Core Idea
Power is not merely a sound limb; it is an attribute distinct from the body.

The concept of power (qudra) is central to discussions of human action. In this passage, the author defines power in relation to us and presents two reductive views that later theologians rejected. Let's examine the text to see how the author distinguishes the sound position from these deviations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it, then the English translation. Notice the brief definition and the two opinions that follow.
The passage is short but packed with three positions: a baseline definition and two alternative views.
2
Understand the main definition
Identify the statement about 'reference in power in our right'. The author says that if power refers to soundness of limbs, it's intelligible; otherwise there is dispute. This sets the stage for the debate.
This is a key distinction: the orthodox view does NOT reduce power to limbs, but allows for a different meaning.
3
Analyse the view of Durar and Hisham
Note that Durar ibn Amr and Hisham ibn Salim defined power as 'a part of the powerful' (ba'd al-qadir). They gave examples: power to grasp is a sound hand, power to walk is a sound foot. This reduces power to physical capability.
This view is considered deviant because it denies that power is an attribute distinct from the body.
4
Examine the rejected view
The text then mentions another opinion that power is 'a part of the empowerable' (ba'd al-maqdur), meaning something about the act itself. The author dismisses it as obviously false.
No explanation is given for its falsity; it is considered self-evidently wrong.
5
Reflect and summarise
Consider why the orthodox Ash'ari position insists that power is an attribute, not a limb or part of the act. Write down your own one-sentence summary of the correct view.
This reflection will help you see why the two alternative views were rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - المقصد الرابع : إثبات القدرة الحادثة وتعريفها حيث قال : المرجع بالقدرة في حقنا إن كان إلى سلامة الأعضاء، فهو معقول، وإن كان إلى أمر آخر ففيه النزاع ) وقال ضرار بن عمرو وهشام بن سالم : إنها أي القدرة الحادثة ( بعض القادر) فالقدرة على الأخذ عبارة عن اليد السليمة والقدرة على المشي عبارة عن الرجل السليمة ( وقيل : ( القدرة ( بعض المقدور ) وفساده أظهر من أن يخفى .
The fourth type - the fourth objective: proof of the created power and its definition. He said: The reference in power in our right if it refers to soundness of limbs, then it is intelligible, and if it refers to something else, then there is dispute. And Durar ibn Amr and Hisham ibn Salim said: it [i.e., the created power] is a part of the powerful (ba'd al-qadir). So the power to take is an expression of the sound hand, and the power to walk is an expression of the sound foot. And it was said: power is a part of the empowerable (ba'd al-maqdur), and its falsity is more apparent than to be hidden.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of power (al-qudra) in relation to human beings and presents two minority views. The text begins by stating that if we refer power in our right to soundness of limbs, then that is understandable, but if it refers to something else, then there is dispute. It then reports the opinion of Durar ibn Amr and Hisham ibn Salim, who held that power is a part of the powerful (some attribute of the agent). For example, the power to grasp is simply a sound hand, and the power to walk is simply a sound foot. This view reduces power to mere physical capability. Then it mentions another opinion that power is a part of the empowerable (something about the act). The text dismisses this view as obviously false.

Key Terms
القدرة
ضرار بن عمرو
هشام بن سالم
بعض القادر
بعض المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power is identical to the soundness of a limb; e.g., the power to grasp is simply a healthy hand.
Correction: The Ash'ari view distinguishes power as an attribute that is not reducible to bodily health, otherwise amputees would lack any power.

Checkpoint
According to Durar ibn Amr and Hisham ibn Salim, what is power?
A) An attribute separable from the body
B) A part of the powerful, e.g., a sound hand for grasping
C) A part of the empowerable act
D) A spiritual capability

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position distinguishes power as an attribute, not reducible to limbs or acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something difficult, do you ever feel that your ability is simply the strength of your body, or do you recognize an invisible gift from Allah? In the next 24 hours, try to catch yourself attributing success to your own limbs alone and consciously shift your gratitude to the One who granted you the capacity.
?
The deviant views in this lesson reduce power to either limbs or actions. Have you ever acted as if your power comes solely from your physical capabilities, ignoring the Creator who sustains them? What specific change in your daily routine could remind you that your power is a borrowed attribute?
?
This lesson teaches that power is an attribute distinct from the act. Is there an area of your life where you feel powerless, perhaps because you think your physical or mental abilities are 'just not enough'? How would your perspective shift if you believed that Allah can grant you a power beyond your current capacity? Commit to making dua for one such area today.
