Why Health is Not a Psychological Quality
The author argues that neither disease nor health is a psychological quality by analyzing their components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that health cannot be a psychological quality because disease, which is its opposite, is composed of non-psychological components.

Core Idea
Health is no more a psychological quality than the disease it opposes.

The author tackles a subtle question: Is health a psychological quality? Since psychological qualities are those perceived by the soul, health might seem like one because it involves the body's ability to act. But the author argues step-by-step that neither disease nor health fit the category. This passage shows the careful reasoning of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand disease classification
Read the passage to learn the three types of disease: bad temperament (saw' al-mizaj), bad composition (saw' al-tarkib), and discontinuity of connection (tafarruq al-ittisal). Note the subdivisions of bad composition.
The author emphasizes that these types are the components of disease.
2
Analyze each component
For each disease type, examine why it is not a psychological quality. Bad temperament is a sensible quality; bad composition involves quantities, shapes, or positions; discontinuity is a privation. None are psychological.
3
Follow the logical chain
If the components of disease are not psychological, then disease itself cannot be psychological. And health is either the existential opposite or the privation of disease, so health also cannot be psychological.
The author addresses an objection about restricting the analysis to position, but it suffices.
4
Consider the conclusion
The author concludes that unless new qualities are proven, health is not a psychological quality. Reflect on how this fits into broader kalam discussions about attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الخامس - المقصد الثاني : الصحة هي ملكة أو حالة
سوء المزاج من المحسوسات، وأما سوء التركيب فهو عبارة عن مقدار أو عدد أو شكل أو وضع أو انسداد مجرى يخل بالأفعال، وليس شيء منها من الكيفيات النفسانية وهو ظاهر، وكون هذه الأمور غريبة منافرة من قبيل المضاف واتصاف البدن بها من مقولة أن ينفعل، واقتصر المصنف من بينها على اعتبار الوضع فعد سوء التركيب منه، وأما تفرق الاتصال فظاهر أنه أمر عدمي فلا يكون كيفية نفسانية، ومنهم من أجاب عن ذلك بأن عبارة الأطباء فيها مسامحة، والمقصود أن أنواع المرض كيفيات نفسانية غير معتدلة تابعة للأمور المذكورة ومخلة بالأفعال ( ولا شيء منها ) أي من الكيفيات المحسوسات والوضع والعدم ) بكيفية نفسانية فلا يكون شيء من سوء المزاج وسوء التركيب وتفرق الاتصال من الكيفيات النفسانية، فلا يكون المرض الذي هو جنسها منها أيضاً فلا تكون الصحة منها أيضاً، لأنها تكون عبارة إما عن أمور وجودية مقابلة للأمور التي سميناها مرضاً وهي المزاج الملائم والهيئة الملائمة
أقسام خمسة، والجواب لا نسلم أن الاقتصار مخل لأنه يكفي لعدم كون المرض مطلقاً كيفية نفسانية أن لا يكون قسم من الأقسام داخلاً فيها.
قوله : ( عن مقدار كالسمن المفرط أو عدد كزيادة أصبع أو شكل كنقطة الرأس أو وضع كزوال عضو عن موضعه أو انسداد مجری کانسداد مجرى الروح الحيواني .
قوله : ( يخل بالأفعال) صفة لكل واحد من الخمسة .
قوله : ( واقتصر المصنف إلخ لكفايته في توجيه السؤال .
قوله: (والمقصود إلخ يدل على ذلك ما في القانون من أن أجناس الأمراض المفردة ثلاثة : جنس يتبع سوء المزاج وجنس يتبع سوء التركيب وجنس يتبع تفرق الاتصال، وفيه أن ثبوت كيفيات نفسانية غير الأمور المذكورة، مما لم يقم عليه شبهة فضلاً عن حجة، ولذا قال الشيخ : إن المرض عدم الصخة على ما سيجيء.
قوله : ( وليس شيء منها من الكيفيات النفسانية وهو ظاهر لأن المقدار والعدد من مقولة الكم، والشكل من الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات كما صرح به نفسه في المباحث المشرقية والوضع مقولة برأسه والانسداد عن مقولة الوضع كما صرح به الأبهري . قوله : ( واقتصر المصنف من بينها على اعتبار (الوضع واعتذر الأبهري عنه بأنه لم يورد الأمور المحتملة في كل قسم منها لظهور بطلانها، ورد بأن قولنا سوء التركيب إما كذا وإما كذا، ليس بياناً للمحتملات بل للأقسام.
قوله : ( فظاهر أنه عدمي قيل : الظاهر أنه إن أريد بتفرق الاتصال المعنى المصدري فهو
انفعال، وإن أريد الحاصل بالمصدر فهو أمر عدمي .
والاتصال الملائم، وإما عن أمور عدمية هي عدم تلك الأشياء المسماة بالمرض، وعلى التقديرين لم تكن الصحة كيفية نفسانية، اللهم إلا إذا ثبت أن هناك كيفيات أخر مغايرة لتلك الوجوديات وهذه العدميات وجعل الصحة عبارة عنها، لكن ذلك مما لم يقم عليه شبهة فضلا عن حجة
The fifth type - the second aim: health is a disposition (malaka) or a state (hala). Bad temperament is among the sensibles. As for bad composition, it is an expression of quantity or number or shape or position or blockage of a passage that impairs actions, and none of these are psychological qualities (kayfiyyat nafsaniyya) and it is obvious. That these matters are strange and repugnant is from the category of relation (al-mudaf), and the body's being described by them is from the category of being affected (an yanfa'il). The author restricted himself among them to considering position and thus counted bad composition as from it. As for discontinuity of connection, it is clearly a privative matter and thus is not a psychological quality. Some answered that the physicians' expression is loose, and the intent is that the types of disease are psychological qualities that are unbalanced, following the mentioned matters, and impairing actions. (And none of them) meaning from the sensibles, position, and privation (is a psychological quality), so nothing of bad temperament, bad composition, and discontinuity of connection is from the psychological qualities. Thus disease, which is their genus, is not from them either, and thus health is also not from them, because it is either an expression of existential things opposite to the things we called disease—namely compatible temperament, compatible form, and compatible connection—or of privative things which are the absence of those things called disease. On both suppositions, health is not a psychological quality, unless it is established that there are other qualities different from those existential things and these privative things and health is made an expression of them, but that is something for which not even a suspicion, let alone a proof, has been established. Five divisions. The answer: we do not grant that the restriction is harmful, because it suffices for disease absolutely not being a psychological quality that no division enters into it. His saying: 'about quantity like excessive fatness, or number like an extra finger, or shape like a pointed head, or position like a limb leaving its place, or blockage of a passage like blockage of the passage of the animal spirit.' His saying: 'impairs actions' is a description of each of the five. His saying: 'and the author restricted himself etc.' because it suffices in directing the question. His saying: 'and the intent etc.' is indicated by what is in al-Qanun that the genera of simple diseases are three: a genus following bad temperament, a genus following bad composition, and a genus following discontinuity of connection. In it is that the establishment of psychological qualities other than the mentioned matters is something for which not even a suspicion, let alone a proof, has been established. Hence the Shaykh said: disease is the absence of health, as will come. His saying: 'and none of them is from the psychological qualities, and it is obvious because quantity and number are from the category of quantity, shape is from the qualities specific to quantities as he himself clarified in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, position is a category by itself, and blockage is from the category of position as al-Abhari clarified.' His saying: 'and the author restricted himself among them to considering position' and al-Abhari excused him by saying he did not mention the possible matters in each division because their falsity is obvious, and it was refuted by saying our statement 'bad composition is either such and such' is not an explanation of possibilities but of divisions. His saying: 'it is clearly privative.' It was said: the apparent is that if by discontinuity of connection the verbal meaning is intended, it is passivity, and if the product of the verbal noun is intended, it is a privative matter. And compatible connection. Or it is about privative things which are the absence of those things called disease. On both suppositions, health is not a psychological quality, unless it is established that there are other qualities different from those existential things and these privative things and health is made an expression of them, but that is something for which not even a suspicion, let alone a proof, has been established.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a classification and argument. The author begins by defining health as either a disposition (malaka) or a state (hala). Then he examines disease types: bad temperament (saw' al-mizaj) is a sensible quality; bad composition (saw' al-tarkib) is explained in terms of quantity, number, shape, position, or blockage—all of which are not psychological qualities. Discontinuity of connection (tafarruq al-ittisal) is a privation. He notes that some physicians speak loosely, but the real point is that disease types are unbalanced psychological qualities that follow these physical matters. However, he insists that none of these physical matters themselves are psychological qualities, so disease as a genus is not a psychological quality, and thus health, being either the opposite existential or privative of disease, is also not a psychological quality. He concludes that unless some other quality is proven, this stands. He then addresses an objection about restriction (al-iqtisar), answering that it suffices that not all divisions are psychological. The rest of the passage includes glosses explaining the subdivisions of bad composition, the reference to al-Qanun, and the categories of quantity, position, etc. The overall argument follows a clear logical chain: disease's components are not psychological, so disease is not psychological; health is defined in terms of disease, so it is also not psychological.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Health is a psychological quality because it involves the body's ability to perform actions.
Correction: Health is not a psychological quality because its opposite, disease, is composed of physical and privative elements that are not psychological; health is merely the absence or opposite of disease.

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that health is not a psychological quality?
A) Because health is a disposition or state
B) Because disease is not a psychological quality and health is its opposite, so health cannot be one either
C) Because health is a privative concept
D) Because the author restricts himself to considering position

Key Takeaway
Health is not a psychological quality because disease, its opposite, is composed of physical or privative elements that are not psychological.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does that challenge the way you interpret your own health struggles or blessings? Will you now separate your physical well-being from your inner spiritual worth?
?
If health is not a psychological quality, then what truly defines your inner state? Could your preoccupation with your body's health be a distraction from the psychological qualities that matter—like faith, sincerity, and humility? What would it take for you to focus more on your soul's health than your body's?
?
The author argues that shape, position, and privation are not psychological. But you experience your body's shape and position daily. Do you subconsciously treat your physical form as part of your identity? How would your self-perception change if you truly internalized that your body's attributes are no measure of your psychological or spiritual reality?

Imam Razi's First Doubt: Why Malaka Precedes Hala?
Imam Razi questions the order of malaka and hala in the definition of health.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two reasons why malaka (disposition) is placed before hala (state) in the definition of health: universal agreement and the mental priority of the final cause.

Core Idea
The agreed-upon takes precedence; the final end is mentally prior.

This passage presents a subtle objection by Imam al-Razi to the standard definition of health. He questions why malaka (disposition) is mentioned before hala (state) when the state logically comes first. The answer reveals two key principles: the importance of agreement and the mental priority of the final cause. Understanding this refines our grasp of how Islamic theologians define technical terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the parenthetical glosses—they are later clarifications.
2
Identify Imam Razi's doubt
Locate the doubt: why is malaka mentioned before hala when the psychological quality starts as a state and then becomes a disposition?
3
Understand the first answer
The first answer: malaka is unanimously considered health, while hala is disputed. So what is agreed upon is given priority.
This follows the principle of preferring the unambiguous.
4
Understand the second answer
The second answer: malaka is the final end (ghaya) of hala, and the final cause is prior in the mind even if posterior in existence.
Final cause has logical priority.
5
Examine the glosses
The glosses refine the answer: one notes the natural order would suggest hala first, but the agreed-upon is more important; another clarifies that malaka is not a final cause but a perfection, hence nobler.
These show how the scholars nuanced the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد الإمام الرازي في المباحث المشرقية على هذا الحد الذي ذكره للصحة شكوكاً وأجاب عنها أيضاً الأول لم قدم الملكة على الحالة في الذكر وإنما تكون الكيفية النفسانية التي هي الصحة أو لا حالة ثم تصير ملكة قلنا : الملكة اتفق على كونها صحة والحالة اختلف فيها فقيل: هي صحة، وقيل: واسطة فقدمت لذلك أو لأن الملكة غاية الحالة والعلة الغائية متقدمة في الذهن وإن كانت متأخرة في الوجود
قوله : (ثم تصير ملكة) فتقديم الحالة أولى ليوافق الوضع الطبع .
قوله : (الملكة اتفق على كونها صحة والمتفق ذكره أهم فلذا قدمه
قوله : ( والعلة الغائية) لا يخفى أن الملكة ليست علة غائية للحالة وإن كانت غائية له بمعنى يترتب عليها، فيتم التقريب، والأوجه أن يقال : الملكة غاية للحالة أي كمال ينسب إليها فيكون أشرف فلذا قدمه .
Imam al-Razi in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya raised doubts on this definition that he mentioned for health and answered them as well. The first: why was malaka (disposition) placed before hala (state) in mention? And the psychological quality that is health is either a state and then becomes a disposition. We said: malaka is agreed to be health, while hala is disputed about it: some say it is health, some say it is intermediate. So it was advanced for that reason. Or because malaka is the final end (ghaya) of hala, and the final cause is prior in the mind even if it is posterior in existence. His saying: 'then it becomes a disposition' — then advancing hala is more appropriate to conform to natural disposition. His saying: 'malaka is agreed to be health and what is agreed upon is more important to mention, so he advanced it.' His saying: 'and the final cause' — it is not hidden that malaka is not a final cause for hala, even if it is a final end for it in the sense that it results from it, so the approximation is completed. The more appropriate is to say: malaka is the final end of hala, i.e., a perfection attributed to it, so it is nobler, thus he advanced it.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Razi's first doubt and its answer. The doubt is: why in the definition of health is malaka (disposition) mentioned before hala (state), when in reality the psychological quality that is health is first a state and then becomes a disposition? The answer gives two reasons. First, malaka is agreed upon to be health, while there is disagreement about hala (some say it is health, some say it is an intermediate). So because it is more important to mention what is agreed, it is placed first. Second, malaka is the final end of hala, and the final cause is prior in the mind even if it is posterior in existence. The glosses then refine the explanation: one gloss notes that if the sequence is from state to disposition, then advancing hala would be more natural; another reaffirms that the agreed-upon should be prioritized; a third clarifies that malaka is not strictly a final cause but a final end or perfection, so it is nobler and thus advanced.

علة غائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The order of 'malaka' and 'hala' in the definition follows the chronological sequence of state becoming disposition.
Correction: The order is based on two criteria: malaka is universally agreed to be health, and it is the final end (perfection) of hala, which has mental priority.

Checkpoint

A) Because malaka is a higher state than hala
B) Because malaka is agreed to be health while hala is disputed
C) Because hala is a type of malaka
D) Because malaka is easier to understand

Key Takeaway
In defining health, malaka is placed before hala because it is universally agreed to be health and because it is the final end (perfection) of hala, which has mental priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The scholars prioritized malaka because it is a final end. What is your ultimate final end—your ghaya? Are your daily 'states' (hala) aligned with that end, or are you stuck in transient concerns? What one change today could realign your actions with your final purpose?
?
When Imam Razi clarified that malaka is a perfection, not a final cause, he elevated its status. Is there a 'perfection' (like a noble character trait) that you are pursuing? Are you treating it as a means or an end? How can you make it your priority?

Resolving Doubt: Subject as Agent, Health as Instrument
Imam Razi's second doubt and its answer regarding dual agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi's second doubt about dual agency is resolved by distinguishing between agent and instrument.

Core Idea
Action's agent is the subject; health is the instrument, not a second agent.

Imam Razi identified an apparent inconsistency in the definition of kalam: it attributes the action of speech to both the subject (who speaks) and the health (the ability to speak). How can one action have two agents? The scholars answer by clarifying that the subject is the true agent, while health is merely the instrument through which the action flows. This lesson unpacks that resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the use of 'from' (عن, من) in reference to both subject and health.
2
Identify the doubt and its impossibility
The doubt is that the definition attributes action to both subject and health, but a single action cannot have two independent agents. Understand why this is problematic.
3
Understand the answer: agent vs. instrument
The answer distinguishes: the subject is the agent (fa'il), health is the instrument (ala). The phrase 'from health' means 'for its sake' or 'by its mediation', not that health is an independent agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني فيه اضطراب إذ أسند فيه الفعل وصدوره إلى الموضوع وإلى الصحة فإن قوله : يصدر عنها الأفعال، يدل على أن مبدأ الأفعال هو تلك الحالة والملكة وقوله : من الموضوع، يدل على أن مبدأها هو الموضوع ولا يكون المسند إليه الفعل بحسب الواقع إلا أحدهما لامتناع صدور فعل واحد من شيئين على أن يكون كل واحد منهما فاعلا له على حدة قلنا : الموضوع فاعل للفعل السليم والصحة آلته في صدور الفعل السليم عنه فقوله عنها أراد به لأجلها وبواسطتها كما أشرنا إليه، وقد صرح بهذا المعنى في التعريف الثاني، وفي الثالث أيضاً، وأما ما يقال: من أن فاعل أصل الفعل
قوله : ( على أن يكون إلخ وما قيل إن الصحة فاعل والموضوع قابل وفيه إشارة إلى أن صدور الأفعال السليمة عن الكيفيات لمدخلية موضوعاتها، ففيه أنه يصح إسناد الصدور إلى القابل على حد كما يدل على إيراد كلمة عن ومن في الموضعين .
قوله: (وأما ما يقال إلخ) هذا مذكور في شرح الملخص وقد نقله الشارح في حواشي حكمة العين من غير حرج وهاهنا قال : ليس بشيء، ولعل وجهه أن السلامة ليست أمراً وجودياً
قوله: (قلنا : الموضوع فاعل والصحة آلته) وقد يجاب أن الصحة مبدأ الفاعل والموضوع قابل ولفظة من في قوله : من الموضوع له بمعنى في كما في قوله تعالى : أروني ماذا خلقوا من الأرض ﴾ [فاطر: ٤٠] ، فالمعنى كيفية يصدر عنها الأفعال الكائنة في الموضوع له .
The second: in it is disorder, because in it the action and its emanation are attributed to the subject (mawdu') and to health. For his saying 'actions emanate from it' indicates that the principle of actions is that state (hala) and disposition (malaka), and his saying 'from the subject' indicates that their principle is the subject. And the one to whom the action is attributed in reality can only be one of them, because it is impossible for a single action to emanate from two things such that each of them is an agent of it independently. We said: the subject is the agent of the sound action, and health is its instrument (ala) in the emanation of the sound action from it. So his saying 'from it' he meant 'for its sake' and 'by its mediation' as we pointed out. And he explicitly stated this meaning in the second definition and also in the third. As for what is said: that the agent of the original action... His saying: 'such that each is etc.' And what was said that health is agent and subject is recipient, and in it is an indication that the emanation of sound actions from the qualities is due to the involvement of their subjects. So in it, it is valid to attribute emanation to the recipient in a certain sense, as indicated by the use of the word 'from' (an) and 'from' (min) in both places. His saying: 'As for what is said etc.' This is mentioned in Sharh al-Mulakhkhas and the commentator transmitted it in his glosses on Hikmat al-Ayn without inconvenience, and here he said: it is nothing. Perhaps its reason is that soundness (salama) is not an existential matter. His saying: 'We said: the subject is agent and health is its instrument.' And it may be answered that health is the principle of the agent and the subject is recipient, and the word 'from' (min) in his saying 'from the subject' has the meaning of 'in' as in His saying: 'Show me what they created from the earth' [Fatir 40]. So the meaning is: a quality from which actions that are in the subject emanate.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents Imam Razi's second doubt and its answer. The doubt is that the definition attributes the action to both the subject and health: one phrase says actions emanate from health (the state/disposition), another says from the subject. This seems impossible because a single action cannot have two independent agents. The answer distinguishes: the subject is the true agent (fa'il), while health is the instrument (ala) through which the action emanates. So 'from health' means 'for its sake' and 'by its mediation', not as an independent agent. This is clarified in the second and third definitions. The glosses then discuss alternative views: some say health is agent and subject is recipient, but the answer maintains that subject is agent and health is instrument. Another gloss mentions that the word 'from' (min) in 'from the subject' could mean 'in' (as in the Quranic verse), so the meaning is 'a quality from which actions that are in the subject emanate'. The passage ends with a note that the claim that the original agent is something else is considered baseless because soundness is not an existential entity.

Correction: The subject is the true agent; health is the instrument, not an agent.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's doubt is resolved by understanding that health is not an agent but an instrument of the subject's action.

Definition of Health and the Priority of Malaka
Defines health and addresses the first objection about priority of malaka.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of health in kalam, the distinction between malaka and hala, and the reasoning behind prioritizing malaka in the definition despite the natural order.

Core Idea
In defining health, what is agreed upon takes precedence over what is disputed, even if it reverses the natural order.

In this passage, the author defines health and immediately faces an objection from Imam al-Razi: why is 'disposition' (malaka) placed before 'state' (hala) when health is initially a state? The answer reveals a key principle of definition in kalam: priority is given to the agreed-upon term and to the final cause. Let's examine this foundational discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of health
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting that health is defined as either a disposition (malaka) or a state (hala), and can be related to appropriate connection or privative matters.
Notice the two possibilities: malaka or hala.
2
Understand the objection
Imam al-Razi objects: why is malaka mentioned before hala when health is initially a state and then becomes a disposition? Consider the natural order.
This objection highlights a key issue in defining terms.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer gives two reasons: (1) malaka is unanimously agreed to be health while hala is disputed, so the agreed-upon term is prioritized; (2) malaka is the final cause of hala, and the final cause is mentally prior.
Reflect on how consensus and causal priority shape definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الخامس - المقصد الثاني : الصحة هي ملكة أو حالة والاتصال الملائم، وإما عن أمور عدمية هي عدم تلك الأشياء المسماة بالمرض، وعلى التقديرين لم تكن الصحة كيفية نفسانية، اللهم إلا إذا ثبت أن هناك كيفيات أخر مغايرة لتلك الوجوديات وهذه العدميات وجعل الصحة عبارة عنها، لكن ذلك مما لم يقم عليه شبهة فضلا عن حجة ) وأورد ) الإمام الرازي في المباحث المشرقية على هذا الحد الذي ذكره للصحة ( شكوكاً) وأجاب عنها أيضاً ( الأول لم قدم الملكة على الحالة في الذكر ( وإنما تكون ( الكيفية النفسانية التي هي الصحة أو لا ( حالة ثم تصير ملكة قلنا : الملكة اتفق على كونها صحة والحالة اختلف فيها فقيل: هي صحة، وقيل: واسطة فقدمت لذلك ) أو لأن الملكة غاية الحالة ) والعلة الغائية متقدمة في الذهن وإن كانت متأخرة في الوجود
قوله : (ثم تصير ملكة) فتقديم الحالة أولى ليوافق الوضع الطبع .
قوله : (الملكة اتفق على كونها صحة والمتفق ذكره أهم فلذا قدمه
قوله : ( والعلة الغائية) لا يخفى أن الملكة ليست علة غائية للحالة وإن كانت غائية له بمعنى يترتب عليها، فيتم التقريب، والأوجه أن يقال : الملكة غاية للحالة أي كمال ينسب إليها فيكون أشرف فلذا قدمه .
The fifth type - second purpose: Health is a disposition or a state and the appropriate connection, or it is from privative matters which are the absence of those things called disease. On both suppositions, health is not a psychic quality, unless it is established that there are other qualities different from those existential things and these privative things, and health is made an expression for them, but that has not been supported by a doubt, let alone a proof. And Imam al-Razi in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya raised objections against this definition which he mentioned for health, and he also answered them. The first: why was 'disposition' mentioned before 'state' in the statement? And it (the psychic quality that is health) is only a state, then becomes a disposition. We say: the disposition is agreed upon to be health, while the state is disagreed upon; it is said it is health, and it is said it is an intermediate, so it was given precedence for that reason, or because the disposition is the final cause of the state, and the final cause is prior in the mind even if it is later in existence. His saying: 'then it becomes a disposition' - then giving precedence to the state is more appropriate to match the natural situation. His saying: 'the disposition is agreed upon to be health' - and what is agreed upon is more important, so he gave it precedence. His saying: 'and the final cause' - it is not hidden that the disposition is not a final cause for the state, even if it is final for it in the sense that it is arranged upon it, so the approximation is complete. The more appropriate is to say: the disposition is the perfection of the state, i.e., a perfection attributed to it, so it is nobler, therefore he gave it precedence.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage defines health and presents the first objection by Imam al-Razi along with its answer. The definition states that health is either a disposition (malaka) or a state (hala), and it can be related to appropriate connection or to privative matters (absence of disease). It argues that health is not a psychic quality unless a special case is proven. The first objection questions why 'disposition' is mentioned before 'state' when health initially is a state and then becomes a disposition. The answer explains that disposition is unanimously considered health, while state is disputed, so the agreed-upon term is prioritized. Additionally, disposition is the final cause (ghaya) of the state, and the final cause is mentally prior. The marginal glosses clarify that despite the natural order (state then disposition), the priority in definition is justified by consensus and by the nobility of the disposition as the perfection of the state.

Key Terms
صحة
ملكة
حالة
عدمية
كيفية نفسانية
العلة الغائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Malaka (disposition) and hala (state) are synonymous and interchangeable in the definition of health.
Correction: The passage distinguishes them: malaka is the final, agreed-upon state of health, while hala is an earlier, disputed stage. Their priority in definition depends on consensus and causal role, not synonymy.

Checkpoint
Why is 'malaka' (disposition) mentioned before 'hala' (state) in the definition of health?
A) Because 'malaka' is more specific than 'hala'.
B) Because 'malaka' is unanimously considered health while 'hala' is disputed, and 'malaka' is the final cause of 'hala'.
C) Because the natural order is 'malaka' then 'hala'.
D) Because the objection required it.

Key Takeaway
In defining health, priority is given to the term that is universally agreed upon (malaka) and to the final cause, even if the natural order (state then disposition) suggests otherwise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own spiritual health, do you define it by the universally agreed-upon practices (like prayer) or by your inner state? Which one do you prioritize—and what does that say about your ultimate goal?
?
The author gives precedence to the disposition because it is the final cause. In your personal development, do you focus on the immediate state you are in or the long-term disposition you aim to build? What would it cost you to shift your focus to the final cause?
?
Think of a deeply held belief or habit you have. Is it grounded in what is commonly agreed upon (consensus) or in a personal, perhaps disputed, understanding? How does the lesson's principle of prioritizing the agreed-upon challenge you to re-evaluate that belief?

Second Objection: Confusion of Agency
Objection and answer on agency in the definition of health.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the definition of health confuses the agent is resolved by distinguishing the subject as the true agent and health as its instrument.

Core Idea
Health is the instrument, not the agent, of sound actions.

This lesson addresses a key objection to defining health as a quality from which sound actions emanate. Al-Razi argued that the definition ascribes the act to both the subject and the quality, creating confusion. The resolution clarifies the relationship between subject and health.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the core of the objection: the definition seems to attribute the act to both the subject and health.
Notice the use of 'من' and 'عن' which imply agency.
2
Identify the key logical premise
The objection states that one act cannot have two distinct agents. Recognize that this is a rational principle in kalām.
3
Study the answer
The response clarifies that the subject is the true agent, while health is merely the instrument (āla) through which the act emanates. Note how this resolves the apparent conflict.
The phrase 'from it' (anha) is interpreted as 'for its sake' and 'by its mediation'.
4
Reflect on the linguistic nuance
Consider how the preposition 'min' can mean 'in' (as in Quran 35:40), which helps reconcile the wording. This shows the importance of philology in kalām.
This is a common method in Islamic philosophical theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني فيه ) أي في الحد ( اضطراب إذ أسند ) فيه (الفعل) وصدوره ) إلى الموضوع وإلى (الصحة) فإن قوله : يصدر عنها الأفعال، يدل على أن مبدأ الأفعال هو تلك الحالة والملكة وقوله : من الموضوع، يدل على أن مبدأها هو الموضوع ( ولا يكون ) المسند إليه الفعل بحسب الواقع (إلا أحدهما ) لامتناع صدور فعل واحد من شيئين على أن يكون كل واحد منهما فاعلا له على حدة قلنا : الموضوع (فاعل للفعل السليم والصحة آلته في صدور الفعل السليم عنه فقوله عنها أراد به لأجلها وبواسطتها كما أشرنا إليه، وقد صرح بهذا المعنى في التعريف الثاني، وفي الثالث أيضاً، وأما ما يقال: من أن فاعل أصل الفعل هو الموضوع، وفاعل سلامته هو الحالة أو الملكة، فليس بشيء إلا أن يؤول بما ذكرناه
قوله : ( على أن يكون إلخ وما قيل إن الصحة فاعل والموضوع قابل وفيه إشارة إلى أن صدور الأفعال السليمة عن الكيفيات لمدخلية موضوعاتها، ففيه أنه يصح إسناد الصدور إلى القابل على حد كما يدل على إيراد كلمة عن ومن في الموضعين .
قوله: (وأما ما يقال إلخ) هذا مذكور في شرح الملخص وقد نقله الشارح في حواشي حكمة العين من غير حرج وهاهنا قال : ليس بشيء، ولعل وجهه أن السلامة ليست أمراً وجودياً
قوله: (قلنا : الموضوع فاعل والصحة (آلته) وقد يجاب أن الصحة مبدأ الفاعل والموضوع قابل ولفظة من في قوله : من الموضوع له بمعنى في كما في قوله تعالى : أروني ماذا خلقوا من الأرض ﴾ [فاطر: ٤٠] ، فالمعنى كيفية يصدر عنها الأفعال الكائنة في الموضوع له .
The second [objection] concerns it, i.e., in the definition there is confusion, because it ascribes the act and its emanation to the subject and to health. For his saying 'acts emanate from it' indicates that the principle of actions is that state and disposition, and his saying 'from the subject' indicates that their principle is the subject. And the one to whom the act is ascribed in reality can only be one of them, because it is impossible for a single act to emanate from two things such that each of them is an agent of it individually. We say: the subject is the agent of the sound act, and health is its instrument in the emanation of the sound act from it. So his saying 'from it' meant for its sake and by its mediation, as we indicated. And he explicitly stated this meaning in the second definition and also in the third. As for what is said: that the agent of the original act is the subject, and the agent of its soundness is the state or disposition, that is nothing unless it is interpreted as we mentioned. His saying: 'such that each is an agent' etc. And what is said that health is agent and subject is recipient, and in it there is an indication that the emanation of sound acts from the qualities is due to the involvement of their subjects, so in it there is that it is correct to attribute emanation to the recipient in a certain way, as indicated by the use of the words 'an' and 'min' in both positions. His saying: 'As for what is said, etc.' This is mentioned in Sharh al-Mulakhkhas, and the commentator narrated it in his glosses on Hikmat al-'Ayn without difficulty, and here he said: it is nothing. Perhaps its aspect is that soundness is not an existential matter. His saying: 'We say: the subject is agent and health is its instrument' and it may be answered that health is the principle of the agent and the subject is recipient, and the word 'min' in his saying 'from the subject' has the meaning of 'fi' (in), as in His saying 'Show me what they have created of the earth' [Fatir:40], so the meaning is a quality from which emanate the acts that are in the subject for which it is a quality.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection raised by al-Razi: the definition of health is confused because it ascribes the act (the sound action) to both the subject (the body) and to health (the quality). The objection argues that one act cannot have two separate agents. The answer clarifies that the subject is the actual agent, and health is merely the instrument (ala) through which the act emanates from the subject. The phrase 'from it' (anha) means 'for its sake' and 'by its mediation'. The glosses further discuss alternative interpretations, noting that the preposition 'min' can mean 'fi' (in), as in the Quranic example. The overall point is to resolve the apparent contradiction by showing that the definition does not posit two independent agents.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Health is an independent agent parallel to the subject.
Correction: Health is merely an instrument (āla); the subject alone is the true agent.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the second objection, what is the role of health in the emanation of sound actions?
A) Health is the primary agent, and the subject is the instrument.
B) Health and the subject are both equal agents.
C) The subject is the agent, and health is the instrument.
D) Health is the recipient, and the subject is the instrument.

Key Takeaway
Health is the instrument through which the subject (the body) produces sound actions, not an independent agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accomplish a good deed, do you attribute it to your health and abilities as if they are the doers, or do you recognize that you (your soul/body) are the true agent and health is merely the instrument? How does this shift affect your gratitude?
?
If health is only an instrument, then even when you are sick and unable to perform certain acts, you are still the agent. What act can you perform right now, regardless of your health, that expresses your role as the subject?
?
Are you treating your health as an end in itself—seeking it for comfort—or as a means to perform sound actions that benefit you in this life and the next? What would change if you saw every moment of health as a loaned instrument?

Third Objection: Circular Definition of Health
Refutes the charge of circularity in defining health.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the charge of circularity in the definition of health is refuted by distinguishing between different types of health.

Core Idea
Defining an abstract concept through a concrete one avoids circularity.

Sanusi addresses a common objection: defining health as 'being sound' seems circular because 'sound' is synonymous with 'healthy'. But the answer shows that the definition actually relies on two distinct aspects of health—one sensible and one not—so no circularity occurs. Understanding this distinction is key to appreciating the precision of kalam definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: 'sound is healthy, so the definition is circular'. Notice the claim that the definition equates the same thing.
2
Understand the answer
The answer distinguishes between 'health of actions' (sensible) and 'health of the body' (non-sensible). The non-sensible is defined by the sensible, avoiding circularity.
3
Reflect on the glosses
The glosses further explain that the technical term 'sihha' is defined by linguistic 'salama', which is not circular. Also note the suggestion to use 'subject' instead of 'body'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثالث : السليم هو الصحيح فالتعريف (دوري أي تحديد للشيء بنفسه حيث عرف الصحة بالصحة (قلنا :) السلامة المأخوذة في تعريف صحة البدن هو صحة الأفعال ) والصحة في الأفعال محسوسة ) معلومة بمعاونة الحس (و) الصحة (في البدن غير محسوسة فعرف غير المحسوس بالمحسوس لكونه أجلى ) فلا إشكال
قوله : ( أي تحديد للشيء بنفسه فالمراد بالدور لازم الدور، وفيه أنه لا حاجة إلى هذا لا يكون المأخوذ في التعريف لفظ السليمة ومعرفتها موقوفة على معرفة السلامة، إذ معرفة المشتق موقوفة على معرفة المشتق منه ولا يخفى ما فيه .
قوله : ( معلومة إلخ) أي المراد أنها محسوسة بالذات .
قوله : (قلنا إلخ) وقد يجاب بأنه عرف الصحة الاصطلاحية بالصحة اللغوية فلا يدور والمآل واحد .
قوله : ( والصحة في البدن غير محسوسة لو قال بدل قوله : في البدن : في الموضوع ليعم النبات، لكان أنسب بالتعريف المذكور.
The third [objection]: 'sound' is 'healthy', so the definition is circular, i.e., determining a thing by itself, since health is defined by health. We say: the soundness taken in the definition of bodily health is the health of actions, and health in actions is sensible, known with the help of the senses, while health in the body is not sensible. So the non-sensible was defined by the sensible because it is clearer, so there is no problem. His saying: 'i.e., determining a thing by itself' means that what is intended by circulation is a necessary consequence of circulation. And in it there is that there is no need for this; the word 'salima' (sound) taken in the definition and its knowledge depends on knowledge of 'salama' (soundness), since knowledge of the derivative depends on knowledge of the derived form, and what is in it is not hidden. His saying: 'known, etc.' i.e., it is meant that it is sensible by essence. His saying: 'We say, etc.' and it may be answered that he defined technical health by linguistic health, so there is no circulation, and the outcome is one. His saying: 'and health in the body is not sensible' – if he had said instead of 'in the body' 'in the subject' to include plants, it would be more appropriate to the mentioned definition.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage addresses the third objection: the definition of health is circular because it uses 'salim' (sound) which is synonymous with 'sahih' (healthy). The answer distinguishes between the health of actions, which is sensible and known, and the health of the body, which is not sensible. By defining the non-sensible (bodily health) through the sensible (health of actions), the definition avoids circularity. The glosses further explain that the technical term 'sihha' is defined by the linguistic term 'salama', which is not circular. Another gloss suggests that saying 'health in the body' should be 'health in the subject' to include plants. The core idea is that defining an abstract concept through a concrete one is valid and does not involve circularity.

Key Terms
دوري
سليم
صحيح
سلامة
محسوس
صحة الأفعال
صحة البدن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using a synonym in a definition always makes it circular.
Correction: Circularity only occurs if the same concept is used without distinction. Here, the definition uses two different aspects of health (sensible health of actions vs. non-sensible bodily health), so it is not circular.

Checkpoint
How does the answer to the third objection avoid circularity in defining health?
A) By using a synonym instead of the same word.
B) By defining non-sensible bodily health through sensible health of actions.
C) By rejecting the definition altogether.
D) By appealing to authority.

Key Takeaway
Defining an abstract concept through a concrete and sensible one avoids circularity in kalam definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to explain your own faith, do you rely on abstract terms that lead to circular reasoning, or do you ground your explanations in concrete, tangible examples from daily life? What is one article of faith you hold that you struggle to define without circularity, and how can the example of health in actions help you articulate it clearly?
?
The passage defines non-sensible bodily health by sensible health in actions. What is an area of your spiritual life that feels abstract or vague? Can you identify a concrete, observable action that directly reflects your inner state, and commit to using that action as a measure of your spiritual health?
?
You likely hold many beliefs that you have never tested against the charge of circularity. Before you finish this lesson, choose one belief—about God, yourself, or others—and ask: 'Is my understanding of this defined by something more clear and tangible, or am I just repeating synonyms?' If it's circular, what sensible anchor can you find to break the loop and make your belief real in your life?

Definition of Disease and Its Nature
Defines disease and discusses the nature of its opposition to health.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars defined disease as the opposite of health and debated whether this opposition is one of contradiction or privation.

Core Idea
Disease is the privation of health, not a positive quality.

Building on the definition of health, this lesson explores the nature of disease. The passage presents disease as the opposite of health, but scholars debated whether this opposition is a simple contradiction or a relation of privation and possession. By examining key texts from Ibn Sina, we see how disease can be understood as a privation in reality, though commonly considered a contradictory opposite. This discussion highlights the nuanced approach of Islamic philosophers to conceptualizing conditions like disease.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the opening passage where disease is defined as a state or disposition from which defective actions emanate. Note that it is presented as the opposite of health.
2
Identify the types of opposition
The passage mentions two types of opposition: contradiction (taddād) and privation/possession (ʿadam wa malaka). Understand the difference.
3
Reconcile the views
The gloss explains that the common view treats them as contradictory, but in reality, disease is a privation. Reflect on why this distinction matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذا الذي ذكرناه من حد الصحة وما تعلق به فالمرض خلاف الصحة ومقابلها فهي حالة أو ملكة يصدر بها الأفعال عن الموضوع لها غير سليمة بل مأوفة وهذا يعم أنواع الأمراض في الحيوانات والنباتات وقد يخص على قياس ما تقدم في الصحة بالحيوان أو بالإنسان وأنت خبير بما يرد على هذا الحد مما ذكره الإمام من عدم اندراج المرض في الكيفيات النفسانية وبأن المرض على هذا الحد يقابل الصحة تقابل التضاد وفي القانون أن المرض هيئة مضادة للصحة، وفي الفصل الثاني من سابعة قاطيغورياس الشفاء مثل ذلك، وفي الفصل الثالث من هذه المقالة السابعة أن المرض من حيث هو مرض بالحقيقة عدمي لست أقول من حيث هو مزاج أو ألم، وهذا يدل على أن التقابل بينهما تقابل العدم والملكة، وفي المباحث المشرقية : لا مناقضة بين كلاميه إذ في وقت المرض أمر أن أحدهما عدم الأمر الذي
قوله : ( حتى يكون لها فاعل، فإنها عبارة عن كون الأفعال على المجرى الطبيعي، فالصادر هو الأفعال الموصوف بالسلامة والفاعل هو الموصوف بالصحة.
قوله : ( مثل ذلك حيث قال : المرض حالة أو ملكة مقابلة لتلك أي للصحة ولا يكون أفعاله من كل الوجوه كذلك بل يكون هناك آفة في الفعل.
قوله : ( لا مناقضة إلخ ) يفهم من كلامه في الشفاء أن التقابل التضاد في المشهور، وتقابل العدم والملكة في التحقيق.
And when you know this which we have mentioned concerning the definition of health and what pertains to it, then disease is contrary to health and its opposite. So it is a state or disposition by which actions emanate from the subject for which it is a quality, not sound but defective. And this includes all types of diseases in animals and plants, and it may be specified analogously to what preceded in health to animals or to humans. And you are aware of what is objected to this definition from what the Imam mentioned: that disease is not included among psychic qualities, and that disease according to this definition is opposite to health by the opposition of contradiction. And in al-Qanun: disease is a form opposed to health. And in the second chapter of the seventh category of al-Shifa', similar to that. And in the third chapter of this seventh article: that disease, insofar as it is disease, is in truth privative – I do not say insofar as it is temperament or pain. And this indicates that the opposition between them is opposition of privation and possession. And in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: there is no contradiction between his two statements, since at the time of disease there is something that one of them is the absence of that which... His saying: 'so that it has an agent, for it is an expression of actions being upon the natural course, so what emanates is actions described as sound, and the agent is described as health.' His saying: 'similar to that, where he said: disease is a state or disposition opposite to that, i.e., to health, and its actions are not like that in every respect, but rather there is a defect in the action.' His saying: 'no contradiction, etc.' It is understood from his statement in al-Shifa' that the opposition is contradiction in the common view, and opposition of privation and possession in reality.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage defines disease as the opposite of health: a state or disposition from which defective actions emanate. It notes that this definition covers diseases of animals and plants. It then discusses objections: disease may not be a psychic quality, and the opposition might be contradiction (taddād) or privation and possession ('adam wa malaka). References to Ibn Sina's al-Qanun and al-Shifa' are given, noting that in one place disease is called a 'form opposed to health' (contradiction), but in another it is called 'privative in truth'. The gloss explains that there is no contradiction because the common view uses contradiction while the deeper analysis uses privation. The definition is thus contextualized within broader philosophical discussions about the nature of disease.

Health and Disease as Principles of Actions
Defines health and disease as action-principles and analyzes their opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how health and disease are defined as principles of actions and the logical nature of their opposition.

Core Idea
Health and disease are not just opposites; their logical relationship can be either privation or contrariety.

This passage from al-Mawaqif defines health and disease as qualities that ground actions. It then examines the logical relationship between them, presenting two possibilities and favoring one, with reference to Avicenna's hesitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the definitions and logical discussion.
Note the mention of Ibn Sina's hesitation.
2
Identify the two definitions and logical relationships
The passage defines health as principle of sound actions, disease as principle of faulty actions. It then presents two ways to construe their opposition depending on which term is called 'disease'.
First case: opposition of non-existence and possession. Second case: contrariety.
3
Understand the conclusion that there is no intermediate
The passage argues that between health and disease as defined (one positive, one privative) there is no middle state because every action is either sound or unsound.
The preferred view is that disease is simply the absence of health.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الخامس - المقصد الثاني : الصحة هي ملكة أو حالة كان مبدأ للأفعال السليمة، وثانيهما مبدأ للأفعال المأوفة فإن سمي الأول مرضاً كان التقابل تقابل العدم والملكة وإن جعل الثاني مرضاً فالتقابل من قبيل التضاد، والأظهر أن يقال : إن اكتفى في المرض بعدم سلامة الأفعال فذلك يكفيه عدم الصحة المقتضية للسلامة وإن أثبت هناك آفة وجودية، فلا بد من إثبات هيئة تقتضيها فكأن ابن سينا كان متردداً في ذلك فلا واسطة بينهما ) أي بين الصحة والمرض المعرفين بهذين التعريفين ( إذ لا خروج عن النفي والإثبات) فالكيفية التي بها تصدر الأفعال عن موضوعها إما أن تكون أفعالها سليمة أو غير سليمة، فالأولى : هي الصحة، والثانية : العدم والملكة بحسب التحقيق، كما يشعر به لفظ في الحقيقة، وقد اختار هذا الوجه للدفع شارح حكمة العين.
The fifth type - second purpose: Health is a disposition (malaka) or state (ḥāla) that is a principle of sound actions, and the second is a principle of faulty actions. If the first is called disease (maraḍ), the opposition is opposition of non-existence and possession; if the second is called disease, the opposition is of the type of contrariety. The more apparent view is to say: if one is content in disease with the absence of soundness of actions, then the absence of health (which necessitates soundness) suffices; but if one posits there an existent ailment (āfa wujūdiyya), then it is necessary to posit a disposition that necessitates it. Thus Ibn Sina seems to have been hesitant about that; there is no intermediate between them (i.e., between health and disease as defined by these two definitions), since there is no exit from negation and affirmation. So the quality (kayfiyya) by which actions proceed from their subject is either that its actions are sound or unsound. The first is health, and the second is non-existence and possession according to investigation, as is indicated by the expression 'in reality'. And the commentator of Ḥikmat al-ʿAyn chose this view for refutation.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage defines health and disease as qualities that serve as principles (mabādiʾ) of actions: health is the principle of sound actions, disease of faulty actions. It then discusses the logical relationship between these two: if we call the first (sound actions) 'disease' (a terminological possibility), the opposition is that of non-existence and possession (ʿadam wa-malaka), meaning one is the absence of the other; if we call the second (faulty actions) 'disease', the opposition is contrariety (taḍād), meaning both are positive attributes. The more evident position is that disease is simply the absence of soundness; but if one insists on a positive ontological entity (āfa wujūdiyya) causing faulty actions, then a separate disposition must exist. Avicenna appears hesitant on this point. The passage concludes that between health and disease as defined (one positive, one privative) there is no middle state, because every action is either sound or unsound. The quality causing actions must be either one or the other; health corresponds to sound actions, disease to unsound actions. The commentator of Ḥikmat al-ʿAyn adopted this view for refutation (of a middle state).

أفعال مأوفة
تقابل العدم والملكة

آفة وجودية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think health and disease are both positive entities.
Correction: The passage shows that disease is often privative (absence of health), and only if an existent ailment is posited is it a positive entity.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the logical relationship between health and disease if the first (sound actions) is called disease?
A) Contrariety (taḍād)
B) Non-existence and possession (ʿadam wa-malaka)
C) Identity
D) Causation

Key Takeaway
Health and disease are defined as principles of sound and faulty actions, with disease typically understood as the absence of health, leaving no intermediate state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider one of your habits or actions. Can you identify the underlying principle (malaka or hāla) that makes it sound or unsound? How would you go about changing that principle?
?
Avicenna was hesitant about whether disease is a privation or a positive entity. Think of a fault in your character: is it simply the absence of a virtue, or is it a positive disposition? How does that affect how you approach correcting it?
?
The passage says there is no intermediate between health and disease—you are either acting soundly or unsoundly. Do you accept this binary? Why or why not? What areas of your life do you try to claim an intermediate state?

Ibn Sina's Refutation of Galen's Intermediate State
Galen's argument for a third state and Ibn Sina's logical refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibn Sina argues that there is no genuine intermediate state between health and disease when the logical conditions of opposition are observed.

Core Idea
Galen's 'third state' vanishes when we apply the conditions of opposition: unified subject, time, and aspect.

In this passage, Galen presents cases like convalescence or partial illness as evidence of a middle state between health and disease. Ibn Sina's refutation, however, shows that such a middle only appears when one neglects the conditions that define true opposition. This lesson will walk you through the argument and its logical foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read Galen's examples
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on the examples Galen gives for the supposed third state: the convalescent, one with some affected organs, one healthy in winter and sick in summer. Note how he claims these are neither healthy nor diseased.
2
Understand the conditions of opposition
Identify the three conditions: unity of subject (the same organ), unity of time (the same moment), and unity of aspect (the same consideration). Why are these necessary for true opposites?
The passage states: 'اتحاد المحل والزمان والجهة'
3
Apply the conditions to the examples
Take each of Galen's examples and apply the conditions. For instance, a convalescent at a given moment: consider a specific organ. Is its action sound or unsound? You'll see it must be either healthy or diseased per that organ.
Under these conditions, a single organ in one time from one aspect is either healthy (sound action) or diseased (unsound). No third option.
4
Explore the verbal dispute
Notice the final part: if one redefines health and disease in extreme terms (all actions always), then the cases become intermediates, but the dispute becomes verbal. Reflect on how definitional choices can change the logical landscape.
Ibn Sina says: 'إلا أن النزاع حينئذ يكون لفظياً.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأثبت جالينوس ( بينهما واسطة وسماها الحالة الثالثة ( فقال : الناقه ومن ببعض أعضائه آفة أو يمرض (مدة كالشتاء ( ويصح مدة ) كالصيف (لا صحيح ولا مريض وأنت تعلم أن ذلك أي إثبات الواسطة إنما هو ( لإهمال شروط التقابل من اتحاد المحل والزمان والجهة و تعلم ( أنه إذا روعي شروط التقابل) بين والمرض فلا واسطة بينهما أصلاً، لأن العضو الواحد في زمان واحد من جهة واحدة أن يكون فعله سليماً أو غير سليم فلا يتصور واسطة بين والمرض المعرفين بما مر إذا روعي الشرائط المعتبرة في التقابل ( وكذا كل متقابلين يمتنع بينهما الواسطة فإنما هو ( أي امتناع الواسطة بينهما ) باعتبار شرائط التقابل ) فإنه إذا أهمل شيء من شرائطه جاز ارتفاعهما معاً، وحينئذ تثبت الواسطة بينهما قال ابن سينا من ظن أن الصحة والمرض وسطاً هو لا صحة ولا مرض فقد نسي الشرائط الي يجب أن تراعى فيما له وسط وما ليس له وسط، وتلك الشرائط أن يفرض الموضوع واحداً بعينه في زمان واحد وتكون الجهة والاعتبار واحداً، وحينئذ إن جاز أن يخلو الموضوع عنهما كان هناك واسطة وإلا فلا ، وإذا فرض إنسان واحد واعتبر منه عضو واحد في زمان واحد، فلا بد أن يكون إما معتدل المزاج سوي التركيب بحيث يكون فعله سليماً، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك فلا واسطة إلا أن يحد الصحة والمرض بحد آخر، ويشترط فيه شروط لا حاجة إليها يعني أن يشترط في حد الصحة سلامة الأفعال، فيخرج سالم البعض ومن كل عضو فيخرج من كان بعض أعضائه مأوفا، وفي كل وقت فيخرج من يصح مدة ويمرض مدة، وأن لا يكون هناك استعداد يقتضي سهولة الزوال فيخرج الناقه والشيخ والطفل ويشترط في حد المرض آفة جميع الأفعال من جميع الأعضاء في جميع الأوقات فتخرج الأمور المذكورة من حده أيضاً وتثبت الواسطة قطعاً، إلا أن النزاع حينئذ يكون لفظياً.
And Galen posited between them an intermediate and called it the third state. He said: the convalescent (nāqih), and one whose some organs have an ailment, or one who is diseased for a period like winter and healthy for a period like summer – neither healthy nor diseased. And you know that that, i.e., positing an intermediate, is only due to neglecting the conditions of opposition (shurūṭ al-taqābul), namely unity of place, time, and aspect. And you know that when the conditions of opposition are observed between health and disease, there is no intermediate between them at all, because a single organ in a single time from a single aspect must have its action either sound or unsound, so no intermediate can be conceived between health and disease as defined previously when the conditions considered in opposition are observed. And likewise, for any two opposites between which an intermediate is impossible, that (i.e., the impossibility of an intermediate) is only with respect to the conditions of opposition. For if any of its conditions is neglected, both may be absent together, and then an intermediate is established between them. Ibn Sina said: Whoever thinks that health and disease have a middle that is neither health nor disease has forgotten the conditions that must be observed for what has a middle and what does not. Those conditions are: that the subject be assumed as one identical thing in one time, and the aspect and consideration be one. Then if it is possible for the subject to be free of both, there is an intermediate; otherwise there is not. And if one imagines a single human and considers from him a single organ in one time, then necessarily either its temperament is balanced and composition sound so that its action is sound, or it is not so; thus no intermediate, unless health and disease are defined by another definition, and conditions are stipulated that are unnecessary. That is, if one stipulates in the definition of health the soundness of all actions from every organ at every time, then some-part-sound, some-organ-affected, and some-time-healthy some-time-diseased are excluded, and also if there is no predisposition that necessitates easy change, then the convalescent, the old, and the child are excluded. And if one stipulates in the definition of disease the ailment of all actions from all organs at all times, then the mentioned items are also excluded from its definition, and the intermediate is definitely established; but then the dispute becomes verbal.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents Galen's argument for a middle state between health and disease, citing examples like convalescence, partial affliction, or seasonal variation. The refutation from Ibn Sina states that such a middle appears only when one neglects the conditions of opposition (shurūṭ al-taqābul): the subject must be identical, time the same, and aspect unitary. When these are observed, a single organ at a given time must produce either sound or unsound action—no middle. Ibn Sina then explains the logical criteria: if the subject can be free of both opposites under those conditions, there is a middle; otherwise not. So, for a particular organ at a moment, it is either healthy (producing sound action) or diseased (producing unsound action) – no third option. However, if one redefines health as soundness of all actions always, and disease as ailment of all actions always, then cases like convalescence become intermediates, but then the dispute is merely verbal (lafẓī).

شروط التقابل
اتحاد المحل
اتحاد الزمان
اتحاد الجهة
مزاج معتدل
نزاع لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Convalescence or partial health is a genuine third state between health and disease.
Correction: It only appears as a middle when conditions of opposition are neglected; under proper logical conditions, each organ at each time is either healthy or diseased.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, why does an intermediate state like convalescence seem to exist between health and disease?
A) Because health and disease are not true opposites.
B) Because the conditions of opposition (subject, time, aspect) are neglected.
C) Because convalescence is a third real state.
D) Because health and disease are both privative.

Key Takeaway
When the conditions of opposition are properly observed, there is no real middle state between health and disease; apparent intermediate states arise from neglecting these conditions or from redefining terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your own life do you create a 'third state'—convincing yourself you are neither fully obedient nor fully disobedient—by neglecting the conditions of truth and accountability that Allah has placed before you? What would change if you honestly applied those conditions today?
?
When you look at your own spiritual health, do you define 'healthy' as complete perfection and 'diseased' as total ruin, thereby excusing yourself into an endless middle? Where is the single organ, the single moment, the single aspect where you must decide: is your action sound or unsound right now?
?
The passage teaches that verbal disputes can mask real ones. In your repentance and tawbah, are you redefining words like 'sincere' or 'effort' to make an intermediate seem inevitable? What would it mean to accept that in this very moment, with this very intention, you are either turning to Allah or turning away—no middle ground?

Resolving Objections in Definitions: The Role of Negation
Responds to an objection and clarifies the negative particle in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple negative construction ('not sound') resolves the objection about the definitions of health and disease and avoids the need for universal quantification.

Core Idea
A simple negation can avoid logical pitfalls in definitions.

This passage from al-Mawaqif deals with a technical objection about the definitions of health and disease. The objector argues that the claim of no intermediate between the two states hinges on how we interpret the word 'actions' in the definitions — universal or generic. The author then proposes a safer definition using the negation 'not sound' to escape the problem. We will unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it, then read the English translation. Notice the Quranic verse and the key terms like 'istiġrāq' and 'jins'.
The verse is used as an example of negative construction.
2
Identify the definitions under discussion
The passage discusses definitions of health (șihḥa) and disease (maraḍ). Health is defined as a state or disposition for actions that are 'sound' (salīma), disease for 'unsound' (ghayr salīma).
3
Understand the objection
The objection claims that the statement 'no intermediate between health and disease' depends on making 'actions' in health definition universal (all actions must be sound) and in disease definition generic (actions in general are unsound). This asymmetry is problematic.
If both are generic, a single organ could be both healthy and diseased.
4
See the proposed resolution
The commentator suggests a safer definition: health is a state for every action that proceeds from a subject that is not sound (i.e., the subject is not sound). The word 'ghayr' in 'ghayr salīma' is a simple negation, not a privation. This avoids the intermediate.
This simpler definition uses negation to avoid universal quantification.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Reflect on how careful attention to logical terms like 'universal', 'generic', and 'negation' can resolve apparent contradictions in definitions and prevent verbal disputes.
The Quranic verse (2:276) shows a similar negative construction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما في قوله تعالى : { والله لا يحب كل كفار أثيم } وأن يكون لفظ الغير في غير سليمة للسلب أي لا سليمة، وإلى ما ذكرنا أشار الشارح فيما سبق بقوله : إذ لم يعتبر فيه إلا كون الفعل الصادر عن الموضوع سليماً لكان أظهر وأسلم، ولو عرف الصحة بأنها حالة أو ملكة لكل فعل يصدر بها عن موضوع لا يكون سليماً لكان أظهر وأسلم . قوله : ( فلا واسطة بينهما قيل : عدم الواسطة مبني على أن يجعل لفظ الأفعال في تعريف الصحة للاستغراق، وفي تعريف المرض للجنس وفيه ما فيه اللهم إلا أن يجعل في كليهما للجنس، ويلزم أن عضواً واحداً يجوز أن يكون صحيحاً ومريضاً إذا كان بعض أفعاله سليماً وبعضها غير سليم.
As in His saying: 'And Allah does not love every ungrateful sinner' (2:276). And that the word 'other' (ghayr) in 'unsound' (ghayr salīma) is for negation, i.e., 'not sound'. And the commentator indicated what we mentioned previously in his saying: 'Since only the fact that the action proceeding from the subject is sound was considered, it would be more apparent and safer. And if health were defined as a state or disposition for every action by which it proceeds from a subject that is not sound, it would be more apparent and safer.' He said: (So there is no intermediate between them). It was said: The absence of an intermediate is based on making the word 'actions' in the definition of health universal (istiġrāq) and in the definition of disease generic (jins), and there is something in that. Unless one makes it generic in both, then it would follow that one and the same organ could be both healthy and diseased if some of its actions are sound and others unsound.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection regarding the definitions of health and disease. The objector argues that the claim of no intermediate (wāsiṭa) depends on interpreting 'actions' (afʿāl) in the definition of health as universal (istiġrāq) – meaning all actions must be sound – while in the definition of disease it is taken as generic (jins) – meaning actions in general are unsound. This asymmetry is problematic. If instead both definitions use 'actions' generically, then the same organ could be both healthy and diseased if some actions are sound and others unsound, thus introducing an intermediate. The commentator responds by clarifying that the negation particle in 'unsound' (ghayr salīma) is a simple negation (salb), i.e., 'not sound'. The safer definition of health is as a state for every action that proceeds from a subject such that the action is not sound (i.e., the subject is not sound). This avoids the need for universal quantification and eliminates the intermediate. The verse from the Quran is used as an example of negative construction.

Misconception: One might think that defining health by 'all actions must be sound' is necessary to avoid an intermediate.
Correction: The passage shows that using the negation 'not sound' in the definition of health avoids the need for universal quantification and resolves the problem of an intermediate.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The passage shows that careful attention to logical terms like universal, generic, and negation can resolve apparent contradictions in definitions and prevent verbal disputes.

?

Definition and Division of Qualities Specific to Quantities
Al-Iji defines and divides qualities specific to quantities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of qualities specific to quantities and their division into two types: those that occur alone and those that occur with something else.

Core Idea
Qualities specific to quantities are either standalone or combined with another attribute.

In this passage, al-Iji introduces a key category of qualities: those that are accidental to quantity. He defines them and then divides them into two clear types. Understanding this division is essential for grasping how such qualities are classified in the framework of kalam, especially for later discussions on physical forms and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice that the passage begins with a definition and then splits into two divisions.
The passage includes some commentary from al-Imam; focus first on the core definition and division.
2
Identify the two main divisions
Look for the phrases 'إما وحدها' (either alone) and 'إما مع غيرها' (or with something else). These mark the two types of qualities specific to quantities.
3
Study the sub-species under 'alone'
Note that 'alone' is further divided into discrete quantities (like evenness/oddness) and continuous quantities (like triangularity/quadrangularity). Observe the examples given.
4
Understand the 'with something else' type
The second type is illustrated with 'al-khilqah' (form), which combines shape (from quantity) and color (another quality). This shows that the quality attaches to quantity in conjunction with another attribute.
The commentator explains that 'with something else' means the quality accompanies quantity like a whole with its part.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you can correctly identify an example of a quality that occurs 'with something else'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الأول : الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات ] ( المقصد الأول (أنها أي الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات ( عارضة للكم إما وحدها فللمنفصلة كالزوجية والفردية) العارضتين للعدد وكذلك الأولية والتركيب وسائر الأعراض الذاتية للأعداد ( وللمتصلة التثليث والتربيع) أي كالتثليث والتربيع فإنهما عارضان للمثلث ،والمربع وكذلك التخميس والتسديس وغيرهما من الهيئات العارضة للسطوح الكثيرة الأضلاع ) وإما مع غيرها كالخلقة، فإنها مجموع شكل وهو عارض للكم) المتصل من حيث أنه محاط بحد واحد أو أكثر (مع اعتبار لون قال الإمام
قوله : ( يكون لفظياً ) أي راجعاً إلى تفسير لفظي الصحة والمرض.
قوله : ( عارضة للكم) أي بالذات .
قوله: (إما وحدها أي منفردة من غير انضمام أمر معه فيكون عائداً إلى ما ذكره الإمام
بقوله : إما لنفسه .
قوله: ( وإما مع غيرها ) أي عارضة لكم مقارنة مع غيره مقارنة الكل مع الجزء ليصح كون الخلقة مثالاً له ويصح كونها قسيماً لقوله : وحدها فإنه لو أجري على ظاهره، ورد عليه أنه كما
هو عارض للكم وحدها عارض له مع كل ما يقارنه ومآله ما قال الإمام : إما لجزئه . قوله: (مع اعتبار لون ) أي لون معتبر معه.
قوله : (مع اعتبار لون أي مع لون معتبر
The First Objective: Qualities specific to quantities. (The First Objective) (They, i.e., the qualities specific to quantities, (are accidental to quantity, either alone: for discrete quantities, like evenness and oddness) that are accidental to number, and likewise primeness, compositeness, and the other essential attributes of numbers, (and for continuous quantities, triangularity and quadrangularity), i.e., like triangularity and quadrangularity, for they are accidental to the triangle and the square, and likewise pentagonality, hexagonality, and other configurations that are accidental to many-sided surfaces. (Or with something else, like al-khilqah [form]), for it is a combination of shape, which is accidental to continuous quantity inasmuch as it is bounded by one or more boundaries, (with consideration of color). Al-Imam said: His statement: (it would be verbal) i.e., referring to a verbal interpretation of health and illness. His statement: (accidental to quantity) i.e., essentially. His statement: (either alone) i.e., isolated without any accompanying thing, so it goes back to what the Imam mentioned with his statement: either for itself. His statement: (or with something else) i.e., accidental to quantity accompanied by something else, with the accompaniment of the whole with the part, to make al-khilqah a valid example and to make it a counterpart to his statement: alone, because if it were taken literally, it would be objected that just as it is accidental to quantity alone, it is also accidental to it with everything that accompanies it, and this boils down to what the Imam said: or for its part. His statement: (with consideration of color) i.e., a color considered with it. His statement: (with consideration of color) i.e., with a color considered.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage defines the category of qualities specific to quantities (الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات). It states that such qualities are accidental (عارضة) to quantity (الكم), and divides them into two types: those that occur alone and those that occur with something else. For discrete quantities (المنفصلة), examples given are evenness (الزوجية) and oddness (الفردية), as well as primeness (الأولية) and compositeness (التركيب). For continuous quantities (المتصلة), examples are triangularity (التثليث) and quadrangularity (التربيع), and by extension pentagonality, hexagonality, etc. The second type is when the quality occurs together with another factor, such as al-khilqah (الخلقة, form or creation), which is a combination of shape (شكل) and color (لون). The commentary explains each term: 'accidental to quantity' means essentially; 'either alone' means without any accompanying thing; 'or with something else' means the quality connects to quantity while accompanied by another, like the whole with its part, making khilqah an example. The initial line about 'يكون لفظياً' is a comment on a previous passage and is not part of the current definition; it is included as it appears in the OCR.

Key Terms
الكيفيات المختصة بالكميات
الكم
الزوجية
الفردية
التثليث
التربيع
الخلقة
الشكل
اللون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The division into 'alone' and 'with something else' is arbitrary; 'with something else' simply means the quality is accidentally related to quantity in any way.
Correction: The division is precise: 'alone' means the quality attaches to quantity without any other accompanying attribute, while 'with something else' means the quality is composed of a quantity-specific part and another part (like shape and color in khilqah), making it a composite accidental property.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a quality that occurs 'with something else' (مع غيرها)?
A) الزوجية (Evenness)
B) التثليث (Triangularity)
C) الخلقة (Khilqah)
D) الفردية (Oddness)

Key Takeaway
Qualities specific to quantities are accidental to quantity and are either standalone (like evenness) or composite with another attribute (like form combining shape and color).
