The Dispute Over Relative Categories
Sanusi states the disagreement on relative categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental disagreement between philosophers and theologians regarding the external existence of relative categories, and why spatial location is a special case.

Core Idea
Theologians deny most relative categories but make an exception for place.

This passage opens the discussion of relative categories by outlining the main fault line. The philosophers (hukama) hold that relations like fatherhood or spatial position exist as real accidents in the external world. The theologians (mutakallimun) reject this, considering them mere mental constructs, with one notable exception: spatial location (al-ayn). Understanding this disagreement is crucial for following the detailed arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Note the concise way the author sets up the dispute.
The passage is only two sentences but packs a lot of information.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify what the philosophers affirm and what the theologians deny. Pay special attention to the exception mentioned.
The exception is spatial location (al-ayn).
3
Reflect on the exception
Think about why spatial location might be treated differently from other relative categories. The commentary will explain.
Consider the nature of place and presence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - مقدمة : الخلاف على إثبات المقولات. أثبت الحكماء المقولات النسبية وأنكرها المتكلمون إلا الأين فإنهم اعترفوا بوجوده وأنكروا وجود ما عداه منها.
The fourth observation - Preface: The dispute over proving the categories. The philosophers affirmed the relative categories and the theologians denied them except spatial location, for they acknowledged its existence and denied the existence of what is other than it.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a preface that introduces the fourth observation of the third standpoint (al-mawqif al-thalith) concerning relations (al-nisab), i.e., the relative categories (al-muqulat al-nisabiyya). It states the disagreement: the philosophers (al-hukama') affirm the existence of relative categories in the external world, while the theologians (al-mutakallimun) deny them, with the sole exception of spatial location (al-ayn), meaning place. The theologians only accept spatial location as a real accident existing outside the mind. All other relative categories are considered mere mental considerations or relations with no external existence.

Key Terms
المقولات النسبية
الأين
الحكماء
المتكلمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Theologians deny all relations, including mental ones.
Correction: Theologians only deny the external existence of relative categories; they accept them as mental constructs. Spatial location is an exception as a real accident.

Checkpoint

A) Place/الزمان
B) Time/الزمان
C) Spatial location/الأين
D) Position/الوضع

Key Takeaway
Theologians only accept spatial location as a real accident; all other relative categories are mental constructs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the theologians affirm only spatial location as a real accident among relative categories, what does that say about how you conceive of God's presence? Are you content that He is not bound by place, or do you secretly wish to locate Him somewhere tangible? Examine your heart: do you try to contain the infinite in a place?
?
The philosophers see relative categories as real; the theologians see them as mental constructs. In your daily life, do you treat relationships like distance, time, and social status as concrete realities that control you, or as mere mental considerations? What would change if you saw them as the theologians do—real only in your mind? How would that affect your reliance on Allah?

The Two Views on al-Ayn
Al-Jurjani states the two views on the cause of spatial occupancy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that while mutakallimun deny most relative categories, they affirm al-ayn (spatial occupancy), and they differ on whether its cause is the substance's essence or a quality.

Core Idea
Mutakallimun agree on spatial occupancy but disagree on its cause.

Al-Jurjani opens by stating the mutakallimun's unique position: they reject most Aristotelian categories but accept al-ayn. He then presents two camps: the majority who attribute spatial occupancy to the substance's essence, and the affirmers of states who attribute it to a quality. This difference sets the stage for a deeper logical debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the two views
Locate where the author states the majority view and then the view of the affirmers of states.
Note the terms kawn and ka'iniyya.
2
Understand the difference in entities
The majority say two things: essence and occupancy; the affirmers say three: essence, occupancy, and the quality that causes it.
3
Reflect on why this matters
The debate is foundational for later discussions on causality and divine attributes.

: ( ربما قال : ( إشارة إلى ما وجد في نسخة أخرى من الأربعين هكذا ( قيام الصفة ) التي هي علة للحصول ( إن توقف على التحيز أي الحصول في الحيز ( لزم الدور) لأنه لما عللنا حصول الجوهر في حيزه بتلك الصفة The First Objective: The mutakallimun's denial of the remaining categories. (First: The mutakallimun, though they denied the remaining relative categories, acknowledged al-ayn and called it al-kawn.) The majority of them hold that the requirement for obtaining in a location is the substance's essence itself, not a quality subsisting in it; so there are two things: the substance's essence and the obtaining in the location, called by them al-kawn. (And a group of them claimed), namely the affirmers of the state, that the substance's obtaining in the location is caused by a quality subsisting in the substance, so they called the obtaining in the location al-ka'iniyya and the quality that is the cause of the obtaining (al-kawn). So there are three things: the substance's essence, its obtaining in the location, and its cause. Imam al-Razi said in al-Arba'in: This is false in our view, because the obtaining of the attribute for the thing means its occupying space dependently on its occupying space; so if it is supposed that the substance's obtaining in the location is caused by the subsistence of another attribute in the substance, then each of the obtaining and that attribute would be dependent on the other, resulting in circularity. The answer is what you have known in the First Observatory of this position, namely that we do not concede that the meaning of 'subsistence' is what was mentioned; rather, it is the descriptive specification. Moreover, even if we concede that the meaning of subsistence is that, we do not concede the necessity of circularity, because it is possible that the essence of the attribute, not its subsistence in the substance, is the cause of the obtaining, and its being spatial (which is its subsistence) is caused by the obtaining, so there is no circularity. His statement: 'He might say' refers to what was found in another copy of al-Arba'in thus: (The subsistence of the attribute that is the cause of obtaining, if it is dependent on being spatial, i.e., obtaining in the location, circularity follows) because when we caused the substance's obtaining in its location by that attribute subsisting in it, the obtaining is dependent on its subsistence in it, and it is supposed that its subsistence in it is dependent on that obtaining, which is circularity. It is replied to by what has passed, that the cause is the essence of the attribute from the perspective of its existence in itself, and it does not follow from this that the obtaining is dependent on its subsistence in the substance. (And if not), i.e., if the attribute's subsistence is not dependent on obtaining in the location, then it is permissible. His statement: 'It is possible that the essence of the attribute' i.e., the attribute itself, as indicated by his saying 'not its subsistence', i.e., it is not that what is meant is the attribute itself irrespective of subsistence. This is based on what the commentator mentioned previously, that the existence of an accident is essentially prior to its subsistence in the subject, as evidenced by their saying: 'Blackness existed, then it subsisted in the body.' Thus it is permissible that the attribute existing in the state of its subsistence is the cause of the obtaining, even if subsistence has no role in causality. However, if the existence of the accident in itself is its existence in the subject, then this answer is not workable, because the existence of that attribute is dependent on the existence of its subsistence in the locus, so the substance's obtaining in the location would be because the dependent occupying-space (al-tahayyuz al-tabi'i) is dependent on the original occupying-space, thus circularity occurs. His statement: 'And it does not follow etc.' even if it is in the state of subsistence. His statement: 'And if it is not dependent' the apparent from the second branch is that what is meant by dependence is the possibility of separation to make the implication correct. The author sufficed with that and chose the first branch and prevented the falsity of the consequent, because the implication is the occurrence of circularity in the sense of each being implied by the other, and that is not impossible. As for the author of Lubab al-Arba'in, he raised an objection. His statement: 'Because it is possible that the essence of the attribute is the cause of the obtaining' involves a research, because if what is meant is that the essence of the attribute before its subsistence in the described is the cause of it, then there is no face for it because it is non-existent then; and even if its existence is conceded, its causing the obtaining of this described in one place rather than another is a preference without a preponderator. And if what is meant is that it, together with its subsistence in it, is the cause of it, that is the truth, because apparently the cause of al-kawn for the spatial being (al-ka'ina) is conditioned by its subsistence in the spatial being. Then circularity arises and another answer is needed.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's position on the category of al-ayn (place/whereness), which they acknowledge while denying other relative categories. The author first states that the majority of mutakallimun hold that the cause of a substance's occupancy of a location (al-husul fi al-hayyiz) is the substance's essence itself, not a quality. Thus, there are only two things: the essence and the occupancy (al-kawn). However, a group of them—the affirmers of states (muthbiti al-hal)—claim that the occupancy is caused by a quality subsisting in the substance. For them, there are three things: the essence, the occupancy (called al-ka'iniyya), and the quality (the cause).

Imam al-Razi then objects, based on his view in al-Arba'in. He argues that the obtainment of an attribute for a thing means its dependent spatial occupancy (tahayyuz tabi'i). If the substance's spatial occupancy is caused by another attribute subsisting in it, then each of the occupancy and that attribute would depend on the other, leading to circularity (dawr). The answer, as previously clarified in the first observatory, is that the meaning of 'subsistence' (qiyam) is not dependent spatial occupancy but rather 'descriptive specification' (ikhtisas na'it). Even if we accept that definition, circularity does not necessarily follow because the essence of the attribute (dhat al-sifa) itself—not its subsistence—could be the cause of the substance's occupancy. In that case, the attribute's own spatial occupancy (its tahayyuz) would be caused by the substance's occupancy, breaking the cycle.

The commentary then explores a variant from another copy of al-Arba'in, where the objection is phrased differently: if the subsistence of the cause-attribute depends on spatial occupancy, circularity arises. The response reiterates that the cause is the attribute's essence, not its subsistence.

Further discussion delves into the nature of the attribute's existence. If the accident's existence in itself is prior to its subsistence in the subject (as argued by some), then the answer works. But if the accident's existence is identical to its subsistence in the subject, the answer fails and circularity would stand. The author of Lubab al-Arba'in raises a similar objection, noting that if the attribute's essence is considered the cause before subsistence, it is non-existent; if after subsistence, circularity returns. Thus, another solution is needed.

The passage follows a clear argumentative order: statement of positions, objection, answer, elaboration, and counter-objections, all focused on the logical structure of spatial occupancy and causality.

كون
حصول
حيز
قيام
اختصاص ناعت
كائنية
دور
تحيز

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Razi's Objection and the First Answer
Al-Razi's circularity objection and the first answer redefining subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's circularity objection against the affirmers of states' view and the mutakallimun's first answer redefining 'subsistence'.

Core Idea
Al-Razi claims circularity; the answer redefines qiyam to avoid it.

Imam al-Razi attacks the view that a quality causes spatial occupancy, arguing it leads to mutual dependency between the occupancy and the quality's subsistence. The mutakallimun reply by denying that 'subsistence' means dependent spatial occupancy; instead it means a 'descriptive specification.' This clever move breaks the circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's objection
Identify the precise argument: if the substance's occupancy is caused by a quality's subsistence, then each depends on the other.
2
Study the first answer
Note that the mutakallimun reject the given definition of qiyam and offer their own: ikhtisas na'it.
3
Evaluate the logical impact
By redefining qiyam, the dependency is broken because the cause (the quality's subsistence) is no longer dependent on the substance's occupancy.

العلية وأما إذا كان وجود العرض في نفسه هو وجوده في الموضوع، فلا يتمشى هذا الجواب لأنَّ وجود تلك الصفة مما يتوقف عليها وجود قيامها بالمحل، فيكون حصول الجوهر في الحيز لأن التحيز التبعي موقوف على التحيز الأصلي فيلزم الدور . قوله : ( ولا يلزم إلخ) وإن كان في حال القيام .. قوله : ( وإن لم يتوقف كان الظاهر من الشق الثاني أنَّ المراد من التوقف جواز الانفكاك ليصح الملازمة، اكتفى المصنف بذلك واختار الشق الأول ومنع بطلان التالي لأن اللزوم صدور الدور بمعنى استلزام كل منهما للآخر، وليس بممتنع وأما صاحب لباب الأربعين فقد أورد قوله : ( لأنه قد تكون ذات الصفة علة للحصول ) فيه بحث لأنه إن أريد أن ذات الصفة قبل قيامها بالموصوف علة له فلا وجه له لأنها معدومة حينئذ، ولو سلم وجودها فاقتضاؤها لحصول هذا الموصوف في المكان دون آخر ترجیح بلا مرجح وإن أريد أنها مع قيامها به علة له وهو الحق إذ الظاهر أن علة الكون للكائنة مشروط بقيامه بالكائن، فقد جاء الدور واحتيج إلى الجواب الآخر.
The First Objective: The mutakallimun's denial of the remaining categories. (First: The mutakallimun, though they denied the remaining relative categories, acknowledged al-ayn and called it al-kawn.) The majority of them hold that the requirement for obtaining in a location is the substance's essence itself, not a quality subsisting in it; so there are two things: the substance's essence and the obtaining in the location, called by them al-kawn. (And a group of them claimed), namely the affirmers of the state, that the substance's obtaining in the location is caused by a quality subsisting in the substance, so they called the obtaining in the location al-ka'iniyya and the quality that is the cause of the obtaining (al-kawn). So there are three things: the substance's essence, its obtaining in the location, and its cause. Imam al-Razi said in al-Arba'in: This is false in our view, because the obtaining of the attribute for the thing means its occupying space dependently on its occupying space; so if it is supposed that the substance's obtaining in the location is caused by the subsistence of another attribute in the substance, then each of the obtaining and that attribute would be dependent on the other, resulting in circularity. The answer is what you have known in the First Observatory of this position, namely that we do not concede that the meaning of 'subsistence' is what was mentioned; rather, it is the descriptive specification. Moreover, even if we concede that the meaning of subsistence is that, we do not concede the necessity of circularity, because it is possible that the essence of the attribute, not its subsistence in the substance, is the cause of the obtaining, and its being spatial (which is its subsistence) is caused by the obtaining, so there is no circularity. His statement: 'He might say' refers to what was found in another copy of al-Arba'in thus: (The subsistence of the attribute that is the cause of obtaining, if it is dependent on being spatial, i.e., obtaining in the location, circularity follows) because when we caused the substance's obtaining in its location by that attribute subsisting in it, the obtaining is dependent on its subsistence in it, and it is supposed that its subsistence in it is dependent on that obtaining, which is circularity. It is replied to by what has passed, that the cause is the essence of the attribute from the perspective of its existence in itself, and it does not follow from this that the obtaining is dependent on its subsistence in the substance. (And if not), i.e., if the attribute's subsistence is not dependent on obtaining in the location, then it is permissible. His statement: 'It is possible that the essence of the attribute' i.e., the attribute itself, as indicated by his saying 'not its subsistence', i.e., it is not that what is meant is the attribute itself irrespective of subsistence. This is based on what the commentator mentioned previously, that the existence of an accident is essentially prior to its subsistence in the subject, as evidenced by their saying: 'Blackness existed, then it subsisted in the body.' Thus it is permissible that the attribute existing in the state of its subsistence is the cause of the obtaining, even if subsistence has no role in causality. However, if the existence of the accident in itself is its existence in the subject, then this answer is not workable, because the existence of that attribute is dependent on the existence of its subsistence in the locus, so the substance's obtaining in the location would be because the dependent occupying-space (al-tahayyuz al-tabi'i) is dependent on the original occupying-space, thus circularity occurs. His statement: 'And it does not follow etc.' even if it is in the state of subsistence. His statement: 'And if it is not dependent' the apparent from the second branch is that what is meant by dependence is the possibility of separation to make the implication correct. The author sufficed with that and chose the first branch and prevented the falsity of the consequent, because the implication is the occurrence of circularity in the sense of each being implied by the other, and that is not impossible. As for the author of Lubab al-Arba'in, he raised an objection. His statement: 'Because it is possible that the essence of the attribute is the cause of the obtaining' involves a research, because if what is meant is that the essence of the attribute before its subsistence in the described is the cause of it, then there is no face for it because it is non-existent then; and even if its existence is conceded, its causing the obtaining of this described in one place rather than another is a preference without a preponderator. And if what is meant is that it, together with its subsistence in it, is the cause of it, that is the truth, because apparently the cause of al-kawn for the spatial being (al-ka'ina) is conditioned by its subsistence in the spatial being. Then circularity arises and another answer is needed.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage presents the mutakallimun's position on the category of al-ayn (place/whereness), which they acknowledge while denying other relative categories. The author first states that the majority of mutakallimun hold that the cause of a substance's occupancy of a location (al-husul fi al-hayyiz) is the substance's essence itself, not a quality. Thus, there are only two things: the essence and the occupancy (al-kawn). However, a group of them—the affirmers of states (muthbiti al-hal)—claim that the occupancy is caused by a quality subsisting in the substance. For them, there are three things: the essence, the occupancy (called al-ka'iniyya), and the quality (the cause).

Imam al-Razi then objects, based on his view in al-Arba'in. He argues that the obtainment of an attribute for a thing means its dependent spatial occupancy (tahayyuz tabi'i). If the substance's spatial occupancy is caused by another attribute subsisting in it, then each of the occupancy and that attribute would depend on the other, leading to circularity (dawr). The answer, as previously clarified in the first observatory, is that the meaning of 'subsistence' (qiyam) is not dependent spatial occupancy but rather 'descriptive specification' (ikhtisas na'it). Even if we accept that definition, circularity does not necessarily follow because the essence of the attribute (dhat al-sifa) itself—not its subsistence—could be the cause of the substance's occupancy. In that case, the attribute's own spatial occupancy (its tahayyuz) would be caused by the substance's occupancy, breaking the cycle.

The commentary then explores a variant from another copy of al-Arba'in, where the objection is phrased differently: if the subsistence of the cause-attribute depends on spatial occupancy, circularity arises. The response reiterates that the cause is the attribute's essence, not its subsistence.

Further discussion delves into the nature of the attribute's existence. If the accident's existence in itself is prior to its subsistence in the subject (as argued by some), then the answer works. But if the accident's existence is identical to its subsistence in the subject, the answer fails and circularity would stand. The author of Lubab al-Arba'in raises a similar objection, noting that if the attribute's essence is considered the cause before subsistence, it is non-existent; if after subsistence, circularity returns. Thus, another solution is needed.

The passage follows a clear argumentative order: statement of positions, objection, answer, elaboration, and counter-objections, all focused on the logical structure of spatial occupancy and causality.

Key Terms
قيام
اختصاص ناعت
دور
تحيز تبعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the only way to avoid circularity is to deny the attribute's causality entirely.
Correction: The mutakallimun show that redefining qiyam or distinguishing essence from subsistence avoids circularity without denying causality.

Checkpoint
What is the first answer given to al-Razi's circularity objection?
A) The attribute's subsistence is not spatial occupancy but a descriptive specification.
B) The substance's essence is the cause, not the attribute.
C) The attribute does not exist.
D) Circularity is harmless.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's circularity objection is countered by redefining subsistence as a descriptive relation, not spatial dependency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you redefined a term to avoid admitting a flaw in your position? How honest was that move? This lesson shows the mutakallimun redefining subsistence—but for them it was a technical, principled distinction. Can you distinguish between legitimate clarification and defensive redefinition in your own life?
?
Al-Razi's objection exposes a potential circularity. What has been the most recent logical flaw in your own thinking that you have refused to admit? If you recognized one today, would you have the courage to say 'I was wrong'?
?
The answer in this lesson relies on careful conceptual distinctions. Are you willing to do the intellectual work to understand and defend your beliefs, or do you prefer comfortable slogans? Commit today to studying one difficult argument in your faith rather than avoiding it.

The Second Answer and the Variant Objection
Further elaboration on the answer, variant objection, and condition about accidental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deeper answer involving the attribute's essence as cause and the condition that the attribute's existence is prior to its subsistence, as well as the variant objection from another manuscript.

Core Idea
The attribute's essence can cause occupancy without circularity if its existence precedes its subsistence.

Al-Jurjani continues the response to al-Razi by elaborating on the second answer: the attribute's essence itself (not its subsistence) can be the cause of the substance's occupancy. He then discusses a variant objection from another copy of al-Arba'in and the subtle condition regarding whether an accident's existence is identical to its subsistence. This lesson unpacks these nuanced distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Study the second answer in detail
Understand how the attribute's essence being the cause, with its subsistence being caused by the substance's occupancy, avoids circularity.
2
Examine the variant objection
See the alternative phrasing from another manuscript that raises the same circularity issue.
3
Grasp the condition about existence
The answer works only if the accident's existence is prior to its subsistence (as some kalam schools hold). If existence and subsistence are identical, circularity returns.

Misconception & Correction

Checkpoint
Under what condition does the second answer (attribute's essence as cause) fail to avoid circularity?
A) If the attribute's existence is posterior to its subsistence
B) If the attribute's existence is identical to its subsistence
C) If the substance's essence is the cause
D) If the attribute is non-existent

The Four Types of Kaun: Sukun, Haraka, Iftiraq, and Ijtima'
Al-Jurjani classifies the four types of kaun and addresses objections from Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu Hashim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of kaun, how sukun and haraka are defined based on precedence relative to another atom, and the objection concerning the atom's first occurrence along with the responses of Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu Hashim.

Core Idea
The atom's occurrence is classified by relation to another atom: sukun is a second occurrence in the same place, haraka is a first occurrence in a new place.

In this passage, al-Jurjani classifies the four types of kaun (state) that an atom can have. He defines sukun and haraka based on whether the occurrence is preceded by another occurrence in the same place or a different place. However, a key objection arises: what about the atom's very first occurrence at the moment of its origination, which is not preceded by any occurrence? This leads to a debate between Abu al-Hudhayl and Abu Hashim. The passage then provides detailed clarifications on the definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of kaun
Read the opening sentence where al-Jurjani lists the four types: sukun, haraka, iftiraq, and ijtima'. Note that the passage then focuses on sukun and haraka, using the basis of occurrence relative to another atom.
The other two types (iftiraq and ijtima') are mentioned but not defined in this passage.
2
Understand the definitions of sukun and haraka
Focus on the conditional definitions: if the occurrence in a place is preceded by occurrence in that same place, it is sukun; if preceded by occurrence in another place, it is haraka. Then see the formal definitions: sukun is a second occurrence in a first place, haraka is a first occurrence in a second place.
Pay attention to the meaning of 'first' and 'second' — they are relative to the atom's history.
3
Analyze the objection about the first occurrence
Read the objection that at the first instant of origination, the atom's occurrence is not preceded by any occurrence, so it is neither sukun nor haraka. This challenges the completeness of the classification into just these two.
This is a critical objection that tests the definitions.
4
Review the responses and clarifications
Study how Abu al-Hudhayl invalidates the exclusive classification, stating the atom is 'kā'in' but neither moving nor at rest. Then note Abu Hashim's view that it is a type of kaun. Finally, read the commentary explaining 'first place' and 'first occurrence' as possibly estimative rather than actual.
The clarifications help resolve ambiguities about the definitions.

Source Text

المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثاني : أنواع الكون

أنواع الكون أربعة هي السكون والحركة والافتراق والاجتماع، وذلك لأن حصوله أي حصول الجوهر ( في الحيز إما أن يعتبر بالنسبة إلى جوهر آخر أو لا، ما لا والثاني ) وهو يعتبر بالقياس إلى جوهر آخر قسمان لأنه إن كان ذلك الحصول مسبوقاً بحصوله في ذلك الحيز فسكون، وإن كان مسبوقاً بحصوله في حيز آخر فحركة ) وعلى هذا ) فالسكون حصول ثان في حيز أول والحركة حصول أول، في حيز ثان ويرد على الحصر على حصر القسم الثاني في الحركة والسكون (الحصول في أول الحدوث أي حصول الجوهر في الحيز في أول زمان حدوثه (فإنه ) كون (غير مسبوق بكون (آخر لا في ذلك الحيز ولا في حيز آخر، فلا يكون سكوناً ولا حركة، فذهب أبو الهذيل إلى بطلان الحصر، وقال : الجوهر في أول زمان حدوثه كائن لا متحرك ولا ساكن ( وقال أبو هاشم) وأتباعه : ( أنه ( أي الكون في أول الحدوث .
قوله : ( في حيز أول ) أي غير مسبوق بحيز آخر بلا واسطة كما هو المتبادر سواء كان سابقاً على حيز آخر أو لا كالجسم الذي حصل في مكانه، ولم ينتقل عنه، وكذا قوله حصول أو غير مسبوق بحصول آخر، سواء كان سابقاً على حصول آخر كما إذا عدم الجوهر بعد الحصول في الحيز الثاني أو لا .
قوله : ( إن كان مسبوقاً بحصوله في ذلك الحيز فسكون وإن كان مسبوقاً إلخ) أراد بالسبق في الموضعين السبق بالاتصال، وإلا فالجسم إذا تحرك من حيز إلى حيز ثم منه إلى الحيز الأول يصدق على الحصول الثاني أنه حصول مسبوق بحصول في ذلك الحيز مع أنه حركة لا سكون وإذا سكن الجسم بعد الحركة يصدق على حصوله الذي هو سكون، أنه حصول مسبوق بالحصول في حيز آخر، وإن كان مسبوقاً بالحصول في ذلك الحيز أيضاً، ولو قال: إن اتصل بحصول سابق في حيز آخر فحركة وإلا فسكون لكان أظهر. قوله : ( فالسكون حصول ثان في حيز أول والحركة إلخ) أولية الحيز السكون لا يلزم أن تكون تحقيقياً بل قد يكون تقديرياً كما في الساكن الذي لا يتحرك قطعاً فلا يحصل في حيز ثان وكذا أولية الحصول في الحركة لجواز أن ينعدم المتحرك في آن انقطاع الحركة فلا تتحقق له حصول ثان، واعلم أن بعض المتكلمين قالوا : الحركة مجموع كونين في آنين في مكانين، والسكون مجموع كونين في آنين في مكان واحد ، ورد عليه بأنه يلزم أن يكون الكون الأول في المكان الثاني جزء من الحركة والسكون معاً، ولا يتمايزان بالذات على أنهم اتفقوا على وجود الكون بأنواعه الأربعة ولا وجود للحركة والسكون على هذا القول عند من لا يقول ببقاء الأعراض
al-Marsad al-Rabi' - al-Maqsad al-Thani: Types of Kaun. The types of kaun are four: sukun, haraka, iftiraq, and ijtima'. That is because its occurrence – i.e., the occurrence of the atom – in a place is either considered with respect to another atom or not. The second (i.e., considered with respect to another atom) is two divisions: because if that occurrence is preceded by its occurrence in that place, then it is sukun; and if it is preceded by its occurrence in another place, then it is haraka. According to this, sukun is a second occurrence in a first place, and haraka is a first occurrence in a second place. An objection is raised against confining the second division to haraka and sukun: the occurrence at the first moment of coming-to-be – i.e., the occurrence of the atom in a place at the first instant of its origination – for it is a kaun not preceded by any other kaun, neither in that place nor in another place; so it is neither sukun nor haraka. Therefore Abu al-Hudhayl held the invalidity of the confinement and said: the atom at the first instant of its origination is kā'in, neither moving nor at rest. And Abu Hashim and his followers said: it (i.e., the kaun at the first origination) ... His statement: 'in a first place' means not preceded by another place without intermediary, as is the obvious meaning, whether it was prior to another place or not, like a body that has occurred in its place and has not moved from it. Likewise his statement: 'occurrence' or 'not preceded by another occurrence', whether it was prior to another occurrence or not, such as when the atom ceases to exist after occurrence in the second place or not. His statement: 'if it is preceded by its occurrence in that place then it is sukun, and if preceded by its occurrence in another place then it is haraka' etc. He intended by 'preceded' in both places the precedence by connection; otherwise, a body that moves from a place to a place then from it to the first place, the second occurrence would be described as an occurrence preceded by an occurrence in that place while it is motion, not sukun. And if a body rests after motion, its occurrence which is sukun would be described as an occurrence preceded by an occurrence in another place, even if it is also preceded by an occurrence in that place. If he had said: 'if it is connected to a previous occurrence in another place then it is haraka, otherwise it is sukun', it would be clearer. His statement: 'so sukun is a second occurrence in a first place and haraka is a first occurrence in a second place' etc. The firstness of the place in sukun does not have to be actual; it may be estimative, as in the case of something at rest that never moves, so it does not occur in a second place. Likewise the firstness of occurrence in motion [is estimative] because it is possible that the moving thing ceases to exist at the instant of the cessation of motion, so no second occurrence is realized for it. Know that some mutakallimun said: motion is the sum of two kauns in two instants in two places, and rest is the sum of two kauns in two instants in one place. This is refuted by the fact that the first kaun in the second place would be part of both motion and rest, and they would not be distinguished by essence, although they agreed on the existence of the four types of kaun, and motion and rest would not exist according to this view for those who do not affirm the survival of accidents.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of kaun into four: sukun, haraka, iftiraq, and ijtima'. It explains the basis: considering the atom's occurrence relative to another atom. If not relative, we consider it alone; if relative, then it is either preceded by occurrence in the same place (sukun) or in a different place (haraka). The definitions are given. An objection is raised: the first occurrence of the atom (at origination) is not preceded by any occurrence, so it is neither sukun nor haraka, which challenges the completeness of the classification. The text then reports the responses: Abu al-Hudhayl argued the classification is incomplete; the atom at that moment is kā'in but neither moving nor at rest. Abu Hashim and his followers considered it a type of kaun. The commentary explains the terms 'first place' and 'first occurrence' in more detail, clarifying that 'first' can be actual or estimative, and notes that some mutakallimun define motion as two kauns in two instants in two places, but this is problematic because it would make the first kaun common to both motion and rest, and because they deny the survival of accidents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the atom's first occurrence at origination should be classified as either sukun or haraka.
Correction: The objection shows that this first occurrence is not preceded by any other occurrence, so it is neither sukun nor haraka. Abu al-Hudhayl explicitly rejects the exclusive classification.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection to classifying types of kaun into only sukun and haraka?
A) The case of combination and separation
B) The case of the atom's first occurrence at origination
C) The case of motion being composite
D) The case of rest being a negation

Key Takeaway
The four types of kaun are distinguished by considering the atom's occurrence relative to another atom, but the case of the first origination reveals a limitation in the simple division into sukun and haraka.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson defines motion and rest in terms of first and second occurrences in places. When you consider your own spiritual life—your 'motion' toward Allah or your 'rest' in sin—do you see a pattern of first occurrences (new acts of obedience) or second occurrences (repetition)? Which do you need to initiate today?
?
The passage highlights that the first moment of an atom's existence is a unique state not captured by motion or rest. Reflect on the moment you first consciously affirmed tawhid. Was that a 'motion' or 'rest'? More importantly, are you still in that first moment of certainty, or have you allowed doubts to move you away?
?
The mutakallimun debated whether motion and rest are sums of occurrences. This shows the depth of their inquiry. How seriously do you contemplate the foundations of your faith? What step can you take today to deepen your understanding of a single aqeedah concept, not just accepting it but truly investigating it?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Motion as a Composite of Rests: The Atomistic View
Defines motion and rest and presents the composition view that motion is a sum of rests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the atomistic kalam definition of rest leads to the conclusion that motion is composed of multiple rests.

Core Idea
Motion is not a single continuous action; it is a series of resting occurrences in successive spaces.

This passage from al-Mawaqif introduces the atomistic analysis of motion and rest. The theologians define rest as a type of kawm (occurrence) that specifies a substance to a particular space. By examining the attributes of two successive kawms in the same space, they conclude that motion must be composed of a collection of rests. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal translation. Let the original wording sink in before analyzing.
Notice the technical terms: كون, سكون, حركة, etc.
2
Identify the key terms and classifications
The passage distinguishes between first and second kawm in a space. It defines the second kawm as rest (sukun). Understand what 'kawm' means as a discrete occurrence.
3
Understand the similarity argument
The passage argues that both kawms in the same space are similar (mutamathilan) because their most specific attribute is identical: specifying the substance to that space. Therefore, if one is rest, both are rest.
This forces the conclusion that the first kawm in any space is also rest.
4
Follow the consequence for motion
Since motion consists of first kawms in successive spaces, and each first kawm is rest, motion becomes a sum of rests. Some theologians explicitly accepted this.
This is a radical atomistic view of motion.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this view differs from the common-sense notion of continuous motion. It shows the power of rigorous definition in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثاني : أنواع الكون (سكون ) لأن الكون الثاني في ذلك الحيز سكون وهما متماثلان، لأن كل واحد منهما يوجب اختصاص الجوهر بذلك الحيز، وهو أخص صفاتهما، فإذا كان أحدهما سكوناً كان الآخر كذلك، فهؤلاء لم يعتبروا في السكون اللبث والمسبوقية بكون آخر فيلزمهم تركب الحركة في السكنات إذ ليس فيها إلا الأكوان الأول في الأحياز المتعاقبة ( ثم منهم من التزم ذلك و ( قال : الحركة مجموع سكنات في تلك الأحياز.
The Fourth Observatory - The Second Objective: Types of Kawm (Rest) because the second kawm in that space is rest, and they [the two kawms] are similar, because each one of them necessitates the specification of the substance to that space, and that is the most specific of their attributes. So if one of them is rest, the other is likewise. These [theologians] did not consider in rest the staying and being preceded by another kawm, so it follows for them that motion is composed of rests, since there is nothing but the first kawms in successive spaces. Then among them is one who accepted that and said: Motion is a sum of rests in those spaces.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage defines two types of kawm (atomic occurrence) and presents a key argument about motion and rest. It begins by stating that the second kawm in a given space is called 'rest' (sukun), and that the first and second kawms in the same space are identical in their most specific attribute—namely, specifying the substance to that space. Therefore, if one is rest, the other must also be rest. The passage then notes that some theologians did not consider the condition of 'staying' (lubth) or being preceded by another kawm in the same space when defining rest. Consequently, they are forced to conclude that motion is composed of rests, because in their view, motion consists only of first kawms (the initial occurrences) in successive spaces. Finally, it mentions that some of them explicitly accepted this conclusion and stated that motion is a sum of rests in those spaces. This passage sets up the debate: whether motion is a simple occurrence or a composite of many rests.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is a single continuous thing.
Correction: In this atomistic view, motion is a series of discrete rests in successive spaces.

Checkpoint
According to the theologians mentioned, what is motion composed of?
A) A single continuous action
B) A sum of rests in successive spaces
C) A combination of rest and motion
D) An instantaneous shift between spaces

Key Takeaway
Motion, in this atomistic framework, is not a continuous action but a sequence of resting occurrences in successive spaces.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your every action is actually a series of discrete 'rests' directly created by God each moment, how does that reshape your sense of accountability for your own movements? Will you begin to see each moment as a fresh divine gift rather than a product of your own momentum?
?
The argument equates the first and second kawm in the same space as identical in essence. Where in your spiritual life have you assumed that 'first movements' (intentions) are different from 'second movements' (actions) when both are equally dependent on God's specification? What would change if you treated them as one?
?
Motion here is composed of many rests. Reflect on a habit or pattern you consider 'moving forward' in your life. Can you identify the individual rests—the small, still decisions that make up that motion? Are you aware of God's presence in each of those still points, or do you only notice the overall direction? Commit to pausing at the next 'rest' to acknowledge Him.
